Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
The algebra of sets is another example of a mathematical system. Notice
the similarity between the theorems (laws) of the algebra of sets and the
theorems of mathematical logic when ∪is associated with ∨, ∩is associated
with ∧, and ′ is associated with ¬. We now prove a few of the theorems stated
above and leave the remainder to be proven as exercises.
Example 3.2.2 Prove the idempotent law: A ∪A = A.
Proof: Let A be a subset in the algebra of sets A with universe U and let
x ∈U.
x ∈A ∪A ⇔(x ∈A) ∨(x ∈A)
Deﬁnition of A ∪A
⇔(x ∈A)
Idempotent law of disjunction
Hence, A∪A = A and by the principle of duality the dual statement A∩A = A
is a theorem also.
■
Example 3.2.3 Prove the identity law: A ∩∅= ∅.
Proof: Let A be any subset in the algebra of sets A and let x ∈U, the
universal set.
x ∈A ∩∅⇔(x ∈A) ∧(x ∈∅)
Deﬁnition of A ∩∅
⇔(x ∈A) ∧f
x ∈∅is a contradiction
⇔f
Contradiction law of logic
This sequence of statements shows that the set A∩∅has no elements. Hence,
A ∩∅= ∅and by the principle of duality the dual statement A ∪U = U is a
theorem as well.
■
Example 3.2.4 Prove the complement law: A ∪A′ = U.
Proof: Let A be a subset in the algebra of sets A and let x ∈U, the universal
set.
x ∈A ∪A′
⇔(x ∈A) ∨(x ∈A′)
Deﬁnition of A ∪A′
⇔(x ∈A) ∨[(x ∈U) ∧(x /∈A)]
Deﬁnition of complement
⇔[(x ∈A) ∨(x ∈U)] ∧[(x ∈A) ∨(x /∈A)]
A De Morgan law of logic
⇔[(x ∈A) ∨(x ∈U)] ∧[(x ∈A) ∨(¬(x ∈A))]
Deﬁnition of /∈
⇔[(x ∈A) ∨(x ∈U)] ∧t
P ∨(¬P) is a tautology
⇔[(x ∈A) ∨(x ∈U)]
A tautology law
⇔x ∈A ∪U
Deﬁnition of A ∪U
Thus, A ∪A′ = A ∪U. Moreover, by the identity law 3, A ∪U = U, so
A ∪A′ = A ∪U = U.
In addition, by the principle of duality the dual
statement A′ ∩A = ∅is a theorem.

Example 3.2.5 Prove the distributive law: A∪(B ∩C) = (A∪B)∩(A∪C).
Proof: Let A, B, C ⊆U and let x ∈U.
x ∈A ∪(B ∩C)
⇔(x ∈A) ∨(x ∈(B ∩C))
Deﬁnition of union
⇔(x ∈A) ∨[(x ∈B) ∧(x ∈C)]
Deﬁnition of intersection
⇔[(x ∈A) ∨(x ∈B)] ∧[(x ∈A) ∨(x ∈C)]
A distributive law of logic
⇔[x ∈(A ∪B)] ∧[x ∈(A ∪C)]
Deﬁnition of union
⇔x ∈(A ∪B) ∩(A ∪C)
Deﬁnition of intersection
Hence, A ∪(B ∩C) = (A ∪B) ∩(A ∪C) and by duality A ∩(B ∪C) =
(A ∩B) ∪(A ∩C)
■
Example 3.2.6 Prove the De Morgan law
(A ∩B)′ = A′ ∪B′.
Proof: Let A, B ⊆U and let x ∈U.
x ∈(A ∩B)′
⇔(x ∈U) ∧(x /∈(A ∩B))
Deﬁnition of complement
⇔(x ∈U) ∧[¬(x ∈(A ∩B))]
Deﬁnition of /∈
⇔(x ∈U) ∧[¬((x ∈A) ∧(x ∈B))]
Deﬁnition of intersection
⇔(x ∈U) ∧[(¬(x ∈A)) ∨(¬(x ∈B))]
A De Morgan law of logic
⇔(x ∈U) ∧[(x /∈A)) ∨(x /∈B)]
Deﬁnition of /∈
⇔[(x ∈U) ∧(x /∈A)] ∨[(x ∈U) ∧(x /∈B)]
A distributive law of logic
⇔(x ∈A′) ∨(x ∈B′)
Deﬁnition of A′ and B′
⇔x ∈A′ ∪B′
Deﬁnition of A′ ∪B′
Hence, (A ∩B)′ = A′ ∪B′ and by duality (A ∪B)′ = A′ ∩B′.

EXERCISES 3.2
1. Let the universe be the set U = {1, 2, 4, a, e, ∅}.
Let A = {a, e, ∅},
B = {1, 4, ∅}, and C = {2, 4}. Write the following sets using roster
notation.
a. A′
b. B′
c. A ∪B
d. A′ ∪B′
e. A ∩B
f. A′ ∩B′
g. (A ∪B)′
h. (A ∩B)′
i. A ∩C
j. (A ∩C)′
k. (A ∩B) ∪C
l. A ∩(B ∪C)

3.3
Additional Set Operations
In this section, we discuss two more binary operations—the diﬀerence of
two sets and the Cartesian product of two sets.
Let A and B be two subsets of the universe U. The set diﬀerence of A
and B, written A−B and read “A minus B,” is the set of all elements in the
set A that are not in the set B. Symbolically,
A −B = {x | x ∈A and x /∈B} = {x | (x ∈A) ∧(x /∈B)}.
For example, if the universe is the set of natural numbers, A = {3, 5, 7, 8},
and B = {1, 3, 7, 9}, then A −B = {5, 8} and B −A = {1, 9}. Observe that
in this example, A −B ̸= B −A. Hence, the operator −is not commutative.
In addition, let C = {5, 9}. Then (A −B) −C = {5, 8} −{5, 9} = {8}.
Furthermore, since B −C = {1, 3, 7}, A −(B −C) = {3, 5, 7, 8}−{1, 3, 7} =
{5, 8}. Thus, for this example, (A−B)−C ̸= A−(B−C). Thus, the operator
−is not an associative operator.
It follows immediately from the deﬁnition of the complement of the set A,
A′ = {x | x ∈U and x /∈A}, and the deﬁnition of U minus A,
U −A =
{x | x ∈U and x /∈A}, that U −A = A′. It also follows easily from the
deﬁnition of the diﬀerence of sets that
a. A −U = ∅
b. A −∅= A
c. ∅−A = ∅
d. A −A = ∅
e. A −B ⊆A

132
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs

Example 3.3.1
Let U = {3, 5, π, e, i, µ}, A = {3, π, i}, B = {5, π, e}, and
C = {i, µ}. Write the following sets using roster notation.
a. A −B
b. A ∩B′
c. (A −B) −C
d. A −(B ∪C)
e. A −C
f. A −(A ∩C)
g. (A ∩B) −C
h. A ∩(B −C)
What do you notice about the answers for pairs a and b, c and d, e and f,
and g and h?
Solution
a. A −B = {3, π, i} −{5, π, e} = {3, i}.
b. Since B′ = U −B = {3, 5, π, e, i, µ} −{5, π, e} = {3, i, µ}
and A ∩B′ = {3, π, i} ∩{3, i, µ} = {3, i}.
Thus, from parts a and b it follows that for the given sets U, A, and B
that A −B = A ∩B′.
c. From part a A −B = {3, i}, so (A −B) −C = {3, i} −{i, µ} = {3}.
d. B ∪C = {5, π, e} ∪{i, µ} = {5, π, e, i, µ}.
So A −(B ∪C) = {3, π, i} −{5, π, e, i, µ} = {3}.
Hence, from parts c and d for the given sets A, B, and C, we have that
(A −B) −C = A −(B ∪C).
e. A −C = {3, π, i} −{i, µ} = {3, π}.
f. A ∩C = {3, π, i}∩{i, µ} = {i}, so A −(A ∩C) = {3, π, i}−{i} = {3, π}.
Thus, from e and f for the given sets A and C, it follows that
A −C = A −(A ∩C).
g. Since A∩B = {3, π, i}∩{5, π, e} = {π}, (A∩B)−C = {π}−{i, µ} = {π}.
h. Because B −C = {5, π, e} −{i, µ} = {5, π, e},
A ∩(B −C) = {3, π, i} ∩{5, π, e} = {π}.
Therefore, it follows from parts g and h that for the speciﬁed sets A,
B, and C, (A ∩B) −C = A ∩(B −C).

From Example 3.3.1, parts a and b, we conjecture that for A and B subsets
of some universe U, it is true that A−B = A∩B′. This leads us to state and
prove the following theorem.

Theorem 3.5
Let A and B be subsets of the universal set U.
Then
A −B = A ∩B′.
Proof: Let x ∈U.
x ∈A ∩B′ ⇔(x ∈A) ∧(x ∈B′)
Deﬁnition of A ∩B′
⇔(x ∈A) ∧[(x ∈U) ∧(x /∈B)] Deﬁnition of B′
⇔[(x ∈A) ∧(x ∈U)] ∧(x /∈B) Associative law of conjunction
⇔[x ∈(A ∩U)] ∧(x /∈B)
Deﬁnition of A ∩U
⇔(x ∈A) ∧(x /∈B)
Substitution A ∩U = A
⇔x ∈A −B
Deﬁnition of A −B
Thus, (∀x ∈U)[(x ∈(A ∩B′)) ⇔(x ∈(A −B))]. Hence, A −B = A ∩B′.
■
Theorem 3.5 shows how to write the set A −B in terms of set intersection
and set complementation. Thus, to prove theorems which involve the diﬀer-
ence of two sets, we may write the diﬀerence of sets in terms of set intersection
and complementation and then use the algebra of sets to prove the desired
result. The proof of Theorem 3.6 illustrates this technique of proof.
Theorem 3.6
Let A and B be subsets of the universal set U.
Then
A −B = A −(A ∩B).
Proof:
A −(A ∩B) = A ∩(A ∩B)′
By Theorem 3.5
= A ∩(A′ ∪B′)
De Morgan law 10D
= (A ∩A′) ∪(A ∩B′)
Distributive law 9D
= ∅∪(A ∩B′)
Complement law 4D
= (A ∩B′) ∪∅
Commutative law 7
= A ∩B′
Identity law 2
= A −B
By Theorem 3.5
■
As we noted earlier, the set {a, b} is equal to the set {b, a}, and it is im-
material which way we choose to write the set. However, the order in which
elements appear and events occur is sometimes very important. Therefore,
entities must be deﬁned for which the order of occurrence of the elements is
signiﬁcant.
By an ordered pair we mean an entity consisting of two elements in a
speciﬁc order. We denote the ordered pair with a as ﬁrst element and b as
second element by (a, b).
In 1921, the Polish mathematician Kazimieri Kuratowski (1896–1980) gave
the following set theoretic deﬁnition of the ordered pair (a, b):
(a, b) = {{a}, {a, b}}.
Using this deﬁnition, the following basic theorem regarding the equality of
ordered pairs can be proved. (See Exercise 21 of this section.)
Theorem 3.7
The ordered pair (a, b) = (c, d) if and only if a = c and
b = d.

134
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
By Theorem 3.7, the ordered pair (2, −3) ̸= (2, 4) because −3 ̸= 4, the
ordered pair (1, 5) ̸= (−2, 5) because 1 ̸= −2, and the ordered pair (3, 6) ̸=
(6, 3) because 3 ̸= 6.
Generalizing the concept of an ordered pair, we deﬁne an ordered triple to
be an entity consisting of three elements in a speciﬁc order such as (a, b, c) in
which a is the ﬁrst element, b is the second element, and c is the third element.
Two ordered triples (a, b, c) and (x, y, z) are equal if and only if a = x and
b = y and c = z. For a natural number n ≥2, an ordered n-tuple is denoted
by (a1, a2, . . ., an) and (a1, a2, . . ., an) = (b1, b2, . . ., bn) if and only if ai = bi
for i = 1, 2, . . ., n.
Let A and B be sets. The Cartesian product of A and B, written as
A × B, is the set of all ordered pairs (a, b) such that a ∈A and b ∈B. That
is,
A × B = {(a, b) | a ∈A and b ∈B}.
The symbolism A × B is read “the Cartesian product of A and B” or often
simply as “A cross B.”
The Cartesian product is named in honor of the
French philosopher and mathematician Ren´e Descartes (1596–1650). In the
process of developing analytic geometry, the synthesis of algebra and geom-
etry, Descartes invented the Cartesian product and the Cartesian coordinate
system. If (a, b) ∈A×B, then it must be true that a ∈A and b ∈B, whereas,
if (a, b) /∈A × B, then either a /∈A or b /∈B.
As an example, let A = {1, a, #} and B = {$, @}; then
A × B = {(1, $), (1, @), (a, $), (a,@), (#,$),(#, @)}
and
B × A = {($, 1), ($, a), ($, #), (@, 1),(@, a), (@, #)}.
As this example illustrates, in general, A×B ̸= B ×A. That is, the Cartesian
product is not commutative.
Let A be a ﬁnite set with m elements and let B be a ﬁnite set with n
elements; then the Cartesian products A×B and B×A both have mn elements
which are ordered pairs. To visualize the Cartesian product A × B we draw a
graph. First, we draw a horizontal line, choose m arbitrary points on the line,
and label the points with the elements in set A. Next, we draw a vertical line,
choose n arbitrary points on this line, and label these points with the elements
in set B. Through each point on the horizontal line chosen to represent an
element of A, we draw a vertical line, and through each point on the vertical
line chosen to represent an element of B, we draw a horizontal line. The
m vertical lines intersect the n horizontal lines in mn points. These points
represent the mn ordered pairs of the set A × B.
A graph of A × B for
A = {1, a, #} and B = {$, @} is displayed in Figure 3.7.

Set Theory

Figure 3.7:
A graph of A × B.
Now let A = {a}, B = {b}, and C = {c}. Then
A × (B × C) = {a} × ({b} × {c}) = {a} × {(b, c)} = {(a, (b, c))}
and
(A × B) × C = ({a} × {b}) × {c} = {(a, b)} × {c} = {((a, b), c)}.
Consequently, in general, A × (B × C) ̸= (A × B) × C. That is, the Cartesian
product is not associative. The Cartesian product of three nonempty sets A,
B, and C is deﬁned as
A × B × C = {(a, b, c) | a ∈A and b ∈B and c ∈C}.
Thus, the Cartesian product of three nonempty sets A, B, and C is a set of
ordered triples.
The following theorem states a relationship between the Cartesian product
and the empty set.
Theorem 3.8
A × B = ∅if and only if A = ∅or B = ∅.
Proof: We will prove this theorem by proving its contrapositive: A ̸= ∅and
B ̸= ∅if and only if A × B ̸= ∅.
First, we assume A ̸= ∅and B ̸= ∅. Thus, there exists an a ∈A and there
exists a b ∈B. Hence, (a, b) ∈A × B and, consequently, A × B ̸= ∅.
Next, we assume A × B ̸= ∅. Thus, there exists an (a, b) ∈A × B, which
implies there exists an a ∈A and there exists a b ∈B. Therefore, A ̸= ∅and
B ̸= ∅.
■

136
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
The following example illustrates combining the Cartesian product opera-
tion with the operations of union and complementation.
Example 3.3.2 Let A = {1}, B = {2, 3}, C = {1, 2}, and D = {3}. Write
the following sets using roster notation.
a. A × (B ∪C)
b. (A × B) ∪(A × C)
c. A × (B −C)
d. (A × B) −(A × C)
e. (A × B) ∪(C × D)
f. (A ∪C) × (B ∪D)
What do you observe about the answers for pairs a and b, c and d, and e and
f?
Solution
a. A×(B∪C) = {1}×({2, 3}∪{1, 2}) = {1}×{2, 3, 1} = {(1, 2), (1, 3), (1,1)}
b. (A × B) ∪(A × C) = ({1} × {2, 3}) ∪({1} × {1, 2})
= {(1, 2), (1, 3)}∪{(1, 1), (1, 2)}
= {(1, 2), (1, 3), (1,1)}
From parts a and b we see that A × (B ∪C) = (A × B) ∪(A × C) for
the given sets A, B, and C.
c. A × (B −C) = {1} × {3} = {(1, 3)}
d. (A × B) −(A × C) = ({1} × {2, 3}) −({1} × {1, 2})
= {(1, 2), (1, 3)}−{(1, 1), (1, 2)} = {(1, 3)}
It follows from parts c and d that A × (B −C) = (A × B) −(A × C)
for the given sets A, B, and C.
e. (A × B) ∪(C × D) = ({1} × {2, 3}) ∪({1, 2} × {3})
= {(1, 2), (1, 3)}∪{(1, 3), (2, 3)}
= {(1, 2), (1, 3), (2,3)}
f. (A ∪C) × (B ∪D) = ({1} ∪{1, 2}) × ({2, 3} ∪{3})
= {1, 2} × {2, 3} = {(1, 2), (1, 3), (2,2),(2, 3)}
From parts e and f it follows that for the speciﬁed sets A, B, C, and
D, (A × B) ∪(A × C) ⊂(A ∪C) × (B ∪D).
■
From parts a and b of Example 3.3.2, we conjecture that for any sets A, B,
and C it is true that A × (B ∪C) = (A × B) ∪(A × C). Hence, we formally
state and prove the following theorem.
Theorem 3.9
For sets A, B, and C, A × (B ∪C) = (A × B) ∪(A × C).
Proof:
(a, d) ∈[A × (B ∪C)]
⇔[(a ∈A) ∧(d ∈(B ∪C))]
Def. of Cartesian product
⇔[(a ∈A) ∧[(d ∈B) ∨(d ∈C)]]
Deﬁnition of union
⇔[(a ∈A) ∧(d ∈B)] ∨[(a ∈A) ∧(d ∈C)]
Distributive law of conjunction
⇔[(a, d) ∈(A × B)] ∨[(a, d) ∈(A × C)]
Def. of Cartesian product
⇔(a, d) ∈[(A × B) ∪(A × C)]
Deﬁnition of union ■
Theorem 3.9 states that the Cartesian product distributes over union.

You are undoubtedly familiar with the geometric representation of the set
of real numbers by the “real number line.” The real number line is usually
drawn as a horizontal straight line. A point is selected to represent 0 and a
second, distinct point to the right of 0 is selected to represent 1. The choice
of the locations of 0 and 1 determine the scale on the number line. It follows
from the axioms of Euclidean geometry that to each point on the line there
corresponds one and only one real number and, conversely, to each real number
there corresponds one and only one point on the line. For x, y ∈R, if x < y,
then the point corresponding to x lies to the left of the point corresponding to
y. Positive real numbers correspond to points to the right of 0 and negative
real numbers correspond to points to the left of 0. If a, b, x ∈R and a < b,
then x satisﬁes the inequality a < x < b if and only if x corresponds to a point
“between” the points corresponding to a and b. A graph of the real number
line is displayed in Figure 3.8.
1
a
0
x
b
Figure 3.8:
The set of real numbers represented geometrically on a line.
We can deﬁne subsets of the real numbers which are intervals using inequal-
ities as follows: Let a, b ∈R and let a < b.
The open interval (a, b) is the subset of the real numbers
(a, b) = {x ∈R | a < x < b}.
The closed interval [a, b] is the subset of the real numbers
[a, b] = {x ∈R | a ≤x ≤b}.
The half-open (half-closed) intervals are
[a, b) = {x ∈R | a ≤x < b}
and
(a, b] = {x ∈R | a < x ≤b}.
Let a, b ∈R and let a < b. The inﬁnite intervals are
[a, ∞) = {x ∈R | a ≤x}
(a, ∞) = {x ∈R | a < x}
(−∞, b] = {x ∈R | x ≤b}
(−∞, b) = {x ∈R | x < b}
(−∞, ∞) = R.
A graph of some intervals is shown in Figure 3.9. The notation ◦means the
real number corresponding to the point is not in the interval; the notation •
means the real number corresponding to the point is in the interval.

3.4
Generalized Set Union and Intersection
The set operations of union and intersection are binary operations. That
is, at present ∪and ∩are deﬁned only for two sets.
However, by using
parentheses, we have been able to calculate more complex expressions such as
(A∪B)∪C, (A∩B)∪(C ∩D), and so forth. Also, until now, we have denoted
individual sets by diﬀerent capital letters. In this section we will extend the
deﬁnitions of union and intersection to arbitrary collections of sets.
Let A1, A2, and A3 (instead of the usual A, B, and C) be subsets of the
universe U. The associative law for set union is (A1∪A2)∪A3 = A1∪(A2∪A3).
Essentially, the associative law says we may omit the parentheses and simply
write A1 ∪A2 ∪A3, since it is immaterial if we calculate A1 ∪A2 ﬁrst and
then calculate the union of the sets A1 ∪A2 and A3 or if we calculate A2 ∪A3
ﬁrst and then calculate the union of the sets A1 and A2 ∪A3. The result of
either calculation is the same. Likewise, the set ((A1 ∪A2) ∪A3) ∪A4 may
be written as A1 ∪A2 ∪A3 ∪A4 and, in general, for n ∈N we may write
A1 ∪A2 ∪· · ·∪An without parentheses and without ambiguity. Note that for
n ∈N, x ∈A1 ∪A2 ∪· · · ∪An if and only if x ∈Ai for some i = 1, 2, . . .n.
Thus, we deﬁne the union of a ﬁnite number of sets A1, A2, . . ., An as follows.

144
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
Let n ∈N and let A1, A2, . . ., An be subsets of the universe U; then the
ﬁnite union of the sets A1, A2, . . ., An is
A1 ∪A2 ∪· · · ∪An = {x ∈U | x ∈Ai for some i = 1, 2, . . ., n}
= {x ∈U | (∃i ∈{1, 2, . . ., n})(x ∈Ai)}.
It is convenient to condense the notation for the ﬁnite union by writing
n
[
i=1
Ai = A1 ∪A2 ∪· · · ∪An.
We extend the deﬁnition of the union of a ﬁnite number of sets to the
inﬁnite number of sets A1, A2, . . . in the following manner.
For each n ∈N, let A1, A2, . . . be subsets of the universe U; then the
inﬁnite union of the sets A1, A2, . . . is
∞

i=1
Ai = A1 ∪A2 ∪· · · = {x ∈U | (∃i ∈N)(x ∈Ai)}.

A set of sets is often called a family of sets, or simply a family. We
will denote a family of sets by a script capital letter. For instance, the family
A = {{a}, {a, b},{a, b, c},{a,b,c, d}} is a family with four sets and the family
B = {(−a, a) | a ∈(0, ∞)} is an inﬁnite family of open intervals. The union
and intersection of a family of sets is deﬁned as follows.
Let F be a family of sets which are all subsets of a universe U. The union

Proof: Let F be a family of sets and let B ∈F. Suppose x ∈T
A∈F A. Then

A∈F A ⊆B.
Let F be a family of sets, let B ∈F, and let x ∈B. By the deﬁnition
of union, x ∈B implies x ∈S
A∈F A, since B is a set in the family F.
Hence, every element of B is an element of the union S

It is tempting to conjecture that if F is a family of sets, then
\

The next example shows this statement is false when the family of sets F is
the empty set. However, the statement T

A∈F A is true whenever
the family of sets F is not the empty set.
Example 3.4.3 Let F be the empty family of subsets of the natural numbers,
N. Show that
a.
\
A∈F
A = N
b.
[
A∈F
A = ∅,
and
c.
\
A∈F
A ̸⊆
[
A∈F
A
Solution
a.
The statement (∀A ∈F)(x ∈A) is equivalent to (∀A)[(A ∈F) ⇒
(x ∈A)].
Since F is assumed to be the empty set, the hypothesis
of the last statement, A ∈F, is false. Consequently, the implication
[(A ∈F) ⇒(x ∈A)] is true for all x ∈N. Hence, T
A∈F A = N.
b.
The statement (∃A ∈F)(x ∈A) is logically equivalent to the statement
(∃A)[(A ∈F) ∧(x ∈A)]. The negation of the last statement is
(∀A)[¬(A ∈F) ∨(¬(x ∈A))] ≡(∀A)[(A ∈F) ⇒(x /∈A)].
Since F = ∅, A ∈F is false, and the implication (A ∈F) ⇒(x /∈A)
is true. Thus, it follows from the deﬁnition of union for a family of sets
that for F = ∅, S
A∈F A = ∅.

Let F be the empty family of subsets of N. Since by part a, T
A∈F A =
N; since by part b, S
A∈F A = ∅; and since ∅⊂N, S
A∈F A ⊂T
A∈F A.
That is, for F = ∅, T
A∈F A ̸⊆S
A∈F A.
■
It is always possible to identify each set in a nonempty family of sets with
an identiﬁcation tag called an index. The following deﬁnition and examples
should help clarify this concept.
Let F be a nonempty family of sets and let I be a nonempty set with the
property that for each i ∈I there corresponds a set Ai ∈F. Then the family
of sets F = {Ai | i ∈I} is called an indexed family of sets. The set I is
called the indexing set and each i ∈I is called an index.
In Example 3.4.1, Ai = [i, i + 1) are the members of the family of sets F,
the natural numbers N is the indexing set I, and each natural number i is
an index. The family A = {{a}, {a, b},{a, b, c},{a,b,c, d}} contains four sets
and may be indexed by the indexing set I = {1, 2, 3, 4} where 1 is associated
with the set {a}, 2 is associated with the set {a, b}, 3 is associated with the
set {a, b, c}, and 4 is associated with the set {a, b, c, d}. Of course, associating
1 with {a, b}, 2 with {a, b, c, d}, 3 with {a}, and 4 with {a, b, c} is another
indexing of the family A by the indexing set I. In addition, the family A
may be indexed by the set J = {a, b, c, d} where a is associated with the set
{a}, b is associated with the set {a, b}, c is associated with the set {a, b, c},
and d is associated with the set {a, b, c, d}. These observations illustrate that
the indexing set for a family of sets is not unique and neither is the indexing
itself. The family B = {(−a, a) | a ∈(0, ∞)} may be indexed by the set
I = (0, ∞) by associating with each a ∈(0, ∞) the interval (−a, a) in the
family B. Notice that B can be indexed by the set (0, ∞) by associating with
each a ∈(0, ∞) the interval (−a/2, a/2). Using this technique, we can clearly
index B in an inﬁnite number of ways.
Every nonempty family of sets F may be indexed. Simply choose the in-
dexing set to be F itself and associate with each set A in the indexing set
the set A in the family of sets. When F is a nonempty family of sets indexed
by the set I, the following alternate deﬁnitions may be used for S
A∈F A and
T
A∈F A, respectively.[
i∈I
Ai = {x ∈U | (∃i ∈I)(x ∈Ai)}
and
\
i∈I
Ai = {x ∈U | (∀i ∈I)(x ∈Ai)}.
Many theorems regarding set operations for ﬁnitely many sets can be
generalized to theorems regarding set operations for arbitrary families.
For example, the distributive laws A ∪(B ∩C) = (A ∪B) ∩(A ∪C) and
A ∩(B ∪C) = (A ∩B) ∪(A ∩C) and the De Morgan laws (A ∪B)′ = A′ ∩B′
and (A ∩B)′ = A′ ∪B′ may be generalized as stated in Theorems 3.11 and
3.12.

3.5
Chapter Review
Deﬁnitions
A set consists of objects called elements or members.
Sets are described in roster notation, in which the elements of the set are
enclosed in curly braces, { }, and separated by commas, or in set-builder
notation, in which the set is speciﬁed in the form {x | P(x)}.
There is one and only one set which has no elements. It is called the empty
set or null set. The empty set is the set ∅= {x | x ̸= x}.
The set A is a subset of the set B, written A ⊆B, if and only if every
element of A is an element of B. That is, A ⊆B ⇔(∀x)[(x ∈A) ⇒(x ∈B)].

Set Theory
151
Two sets A and B are equal if and only if [(A ⊆B) ∧(B ⊆A)].
The set A is a proper subset of the set B, written A ⊂B, if and only if
A is a subset of B and A ̸= B. That is, A ⊂B ⇔[(A ⊆B) ∧(A ̸= B)].
The set of all subsets of a ﬁnite set A is denoted by P(A) and is called the
power set of A.
Let A and B be two subsets of the universe U. The set A union B is the
subset of U which contains all elements that belong either to A or to B or to
both A and B. Thus,
A ∪B = {x | x ∈A or x ∈B} = {x | (x ∈A) ∨(x ∈B)}.
The set A intersect B is the subset of U which contains all elements that
belong both A and B. That is,
A ∩B = {x | x ∈A and x ∈B} = {x | (x ∈A) ∧(x ∈B)}.
When A ∩B = ∅, the sets A and B are said to be disjoint.
The complement of the set A, written A′, is the set of all elements in the
universe, U, which are not in the set A. Hence, A′ = {x | x ∈U and x /∈A} =
{x | (x ∈U) ∧(x /∈A)}.
An equation, expression, or statement in set theory which is obtained by
interchanging ∪and ∩and interchanging the sets ∅and U is called the dual
of the equation, expression, or statement accordingly.
Let A and B be two subsets of the universe U. The set diﬀerence of A
and B, written A −B, is the set of all elements in the set A that are not in
the set B. That is, A −B = {x | x ∈A and x /∈B} = {x | (x ∈A) ∧(x /∈B)}.
An ordered pair with a as ﬁrst element and b as second element is
denoted by (a, b).
The Cartesian product of two sets A and B, written as A × B, is the
set of all ordered pairs (a, b) such that a ∈A and b ∈B. That is, A × B =
{(a, b) | a ∈A and b ∈B}.
Let a, b ∈R and let a < b.
The open interval (a, b) is the subset of the real numbers
(a, b) = {x ∈R | a < x < b}.
The closed interval [a, b] is the subset of the real numbers
[a, b] = {x ∈R | a ≤x ≤b}.
The half-open (half-closed) intervals are
[a, b) = {x ∈R | a ≤x < b} and (a, b] = {x ∈R | a < x ≤b}.
The inﬁnite intervals are
[a, ∞) = {x ∈R | a ≤x}
(a, ∞) = {x ∈R | a < x}
(−∞, b] = {x ∈R | x ≤b}

152
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
(−∞, b) = {x ∈R | x < b}
(−∞, ∞) = R.

= {x ∈U | (∃i ∈{1, 2, . . ., n})(x ∈Ai)}.

Let F be a nonempty family of sets and let I be a nonempty set with the
property that for each i ∈I there corresponds a set Ai ∈F. Then the family
of sets F = {Ai | i ∈I} is called an indexed family of sets. The set I is
called the indexing set and each i ∈I is called an index.
Useful Laws
The principle of duality for set theory says “If T is a theorem which is
written in terms of ∪, ∩, and ′, then the dual statement T D is also a theorem.”
The algebra of sets consists of a collection A of subsets ∅, U, A, B, C, . . .
of some universe U. On the collection A there is deﬁned two relations, ⊆

Set Theory
153
and =; two binary operations, ∪and ∩; and one unary operation ′. The
collection A also has the properties that if A and B are arbitrary sets of A,
then A ∪B ∈A, A ∩B ∈A, and A′ ∈A. For A an algebra of sets, the
following statements and their duals are theorems.

The algebra of sets is an example of a mathematical system.

In Exercises 17 through 20 a statement and a proof? are given.
For each exercise do the following.
a. Determine if the given statement is true or false.
b. If the statement is a theorem, determine if the proof? is valid.
c. If the given statement is false, provide a counterexample.
17. Statement. Theorem? Proof?
Statement: Let A ⊆B and C ⊆B. If x ∈A, then x ∈C.
Proof? Suppose x ̸∈C. Since x ∈A and A ⊆B, x ∈B. Since x ̸∈C
and C ⊆B, x ̸∈B. Because x ∈B and x ̸∈B, we have a contradiction.
Therefore, x ∈C.
18. Statement. Theorem? Proof?
Statement: Let A, B, and C be nonempty sets. If A−B ⊆C and A ̸⊆C,
then A ∩B ̸= ∅.
Proof? Since A ̸⊆C, there exists an x ∈A such that x ̸∈C. Since x ̸∈C
and A −B ⊆C, x ̸∈A −B. Since x ∈A and x ̸∈A −B, x ∈B. Hence,
x ∈A ∩B. That is, A ∩B ̸= ∅.
19. Statement. Theorem? Proof?
Statement: Let A, B, C and D be sets. If A × B ⊆C × D, then A ⊆C
and B ⊆D.
Proof? Let a be any element of A and let b be any element of B. Then
(a, b) ∈A × B. Since A × B ⊆C × D, (a, b) ∈C × D. Hence, a ∈C and
b ∈D. Therefore, A ⊆C and B ⊆D.
20. Statement. Theorem? Proof?
Statement: Let A and B be nonempty sets and let C and D be sets. If
A × B ⊆C × D, then A ⊆C and B ⊆D.
Proof? The proof is the same as the one presented in Exercise 19.


Chapter 4
Relations
Relations and functions play a major role in many branches of mathematics
and sciences. Historically, the concept of a function was introduced prior to
the concept of a relation. However, a relation is more general entity than a
function. Therefore, in this chapter we will consider relations and in the next
chapter we will consider functions.
4.1
Relations
In everyday conversations, we often hear statements such as “Richard is
the husband of Sue,” “Kimberly is the sister of Jerry,” “Alaska is larger than
Arizona,” and so forth. Each of these sentences includes a predicate (“is the
husband of,” “is the sister of,” or “is larger than”) which expresses a “relation”
between two objects. The word “relation” implies an association between two
objects—people, states, numbers, concepts, etc.—based on some property
of the objects. In elementary mathematics, you previously encountered the
relations “is equal to,” “is less than,” “is congruent to,” “is parallel to,” and
many more.
Let P(x, y) denote the open sentence in two variables: “x is
the husband of y.”
If we specify that the variable x is an element of the
set of all living male people, M, and y is an element of the set of all living
female people, F, then given a particular ordered pair (m, f) ∈M ×F we can
substitute x = m and y = f into the open sentence P(x, y) and obtain the
statement P(m, f). This statement will be true or false but not both. Let H
denote the predicate “is the husband of.” When P(m, f) is true, we will write
(m, f) ∈H, which is read “m is H-related to f.” In this instance, we would
say “m is the husband of f.” On the other hand, when P(m, f) is false, we
will write (m, f) /∈H, which is read “m is not H-related to f.” In general, two
sets of ordered pairs are determined by each relation R—the set of ordered
pairs which satisﬁes the relation, R, and the set of ordered pairs which does
not satisfy the relation, R′.
159

160
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
We now state the mathematical deﬁnition of a relation formally. Let A and
B be sets.
A relation from A to B is any subset of A × B. In particular, when
B = A, a relation R from A to A is called a relation on A.
The domain of a relation R from A to B is the set
Dom(R) = {x ∈A | (∃y ∈B)((x, y) ∈R)}.
The range of a relation R from A to B is the set
Rng(R) = {y ∈B | (∃x ∈A)((x, y) ∈R)}.
The domain of a relation R from A to B is the set of all ﬁrst coordinates of
the ordered pairs in the set R and, by deﬁnition, the domain of R is a subset
of A—that is, Dom(R) ⊆A. The range of a relation R from A to B is the set
of all second coordinates of the ordered pairs in the set R and, by deﬁnition,
Rng(R) ⊆B.
Let R be any set of ordered pairs, let A be any set such that Dom(R) ⊆A,
and let B be any set such that Rng(R) ⊆B.
Then by deﬁnition R is a
relation from A to B.
Consequently, every set of ordered pairs is a
relation. For example, let R = {(1, a), (b, 2), (1, 2)}. Then Dom(R) = {1, b},
Rng(R) = {a, 2}, and R is a relation from A = Dom(R) to B = Rng(R).
Example 4.1.1 Let A = {1, 2, 3}, and B = {2, 3, 4}. Let S be the relation
from A to B deﬁned by
S = {(x, y) ∈A × B | x is less than y}.
And let T be the relation from B to A deﬁned by
T = {(x, y) ∈B × A | x is less than y}.
a. Use the roster method to specify S, Dom(S), and Rng(S).
b. Use the roster method to specify T, Dom(T), and Rng(T).
Solution
a. Since 1 ∈A and 1 < 2 ∈B, 1 < 3 ∈B, and 1 < 4 ∈B, the ordered
pairs (1, 2), (1, 3), and (1, 4) are elements of S. Likewise, since 2 ∈A but
2 ̸< 2 ∈B, (2, 2) /∈S. However, since 2 < 3 ∈B and 2 < 4 ∈B, (2, 3) and
(2, 4) are elements of S. Since 3 ∈A, 3 ̸< 2 ∈B and 3 ̸< 3 ∈B, (3, 2) /∈S
and (3, 3) /∈S. But since 3 < 4 ∈B, (3, 4) ∈S. Hence,
S = {(1, 2), (1, 3), (1,4), (2, 3),(2, 4), (3,4)}.
Because the ﬁrst coordinates of the ordered pairs of S are 1, 2, 3, Dom(S) =
{1, 2, 3} = A. Because the second coordinates of the ordered pairs of S are 2,
3, 4, Rng(S) = {2, 3, 4} = B.
b. Since 2 ∈B and 2 < 3 ∈A but b < a is false for all other b ∈B and
a ∈A, T = {(2, 3)}, Dom(T) = {2} ⊂B, and Rng(T) = {3} ⊂A.

and B = {2, 3, 4} is displayed in Figure 4.1(a) and a graph of the complement

If we interchange the components of the ordered pairs in the relation S
of Example 4.1.1, we obtain the set {(2, 1), (3, 1), (4, 1), (3,2), (4,2),(4, 3)}.
This relation is designated by S−1 and is called the inverse relation of S.
Observe that Dom(S−1) = {2, 3, 4} = Rng(S) and Rng(S−1) = {1, 2, 3} =
Dom(S). Now let U = Dom(S) ∪Rng(S) = {1, 2, 3, 4}. Graphing the ordered

Figure 4.2:
A graph of the relations S and S−1.
Notice that corresponding to each point (a, b) ∈S there corresponds exactly
one point (b, a) ∈S−1. For example, (1, 2) in S corresponds to (2, 1) in S−1,
(2, 3) in S corresponds to (3, 2) in S−1, and so forth. If the “diagonal line
y = x” is considered to be a mirror, then corresponding to each point (a, b) ∈S
is the “mirror image” (b, a) ∈S−1 and vice versa. Hence, the relation S−1
is the “mirror image” of the relation S with respect to the “line y = x” and,
likewise, S is the “mirror image” of S−1. Also observe from Figure 4.1(b) and
Figure 4.2 that S−1 ̸= S′—that is, the inverse of a relation is not equal to its
complement.
In set-builder notation, S = {(x, y) ∈A × B | x < y} and, as indicted by
Figure 4.2, the inverse relation S−1 = {(x, y) ∈B×A | y < x}. The condition

162
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
y < x used to deﬁne S−1 is obtained from the condition x < y used to deﬁne
S by interchanging the variables x and y. Thus, if a relation is deﬁned by
some condition on the variables x and y, then the condition which deﬁnes the
inverse relation is obtained from the original condition by interchanging the
variables x and y. In general, we deﬁne an inverse relation in the following
manner. If R is a relation from A to B, then the inverse relation from B
to A is the relation
R−1 = {(x, y) ∈B × A | (y, x) ∈R}.
An important family of relations is the family of identity relations—one
relation for each nonempty set A. This family is deﬁned as follows. Let A be
a nonempty set. The identity relation on A is the set
IA = {(x, x) ∈A × A | x ∈A}.
From the deﬁnition of the identity relation, it is clear that Dom(IA) =
Rng(IA) = A. If C = {1, 2, 3, 4}, then IC = {(1, 1), (2, 2), (3, 3), (4,4)}. A
graph of IC is shown in Figure 4.3. The points on IC are points on the “line”
about which a relation S on C is reﬂected in order to obtain the inverse rela-
tion S−1 on C. (Locate the points of the relation IC in Figure 4.2 and notice
that the point (1, 3) is the reﬂection of (3, 1) about the point (2, 2) ∈IC and
that the point (2, 4) is the reﬂection of (4, 2) about the point (3, 3) ∈IC.)

Let R be a relation from A to B and let S be a relation from C to D.
The relation R equals S, which is denoted by R = S, if and only if A = C,
B = D, and [(x, y) ∈R ⇔(x, y) ∈S].
The next two examples should help clarify this deﬁnition. The identity
relation on the natural numbers is
IN = {(x, x) ∈N × N | x = x}
and the identity relation on the integers is
IZ = {(x, x) ∈Z × Z | x = x}.
The identity relation IN ̸= IZ, because Dom(IN) = N ̸= Z = Dom(IZ).
Let A = {1, 2} = C, B = {a, b} = D, R = {(1, a), (2, b)}, and S = {(1, b),
(2, a)}. Then Dom(R) = {1, 2} = Dom(S) and Rng(R) = {a, b} = Rng(S);
however, R ̸= S because (1, a) ∈R but (1, a) /∈S.

Relations
163
Since relations are sets, we can perform the usual unary set operation of
complementation and the binary set operations of union, intersection, and set
diﬀerence on relations. Moreover, because relations are sets of ordered pairs,
we can perform additional binary operations such as composition. Let R be a
relation from A to B and let S be a relation from B to C. The composition
of S and R is the relation
S ◦R = {(a, c) ∈A × C | (∃b ∈B)[((a, b) ∈R) ∧((b, c) ∈S)]}.
Example 4.1.2
Let A = {a, b, c, d}, B = {1, 2, 3, 4,5}, C = {w, x, y, z},
R = {(a, 1), (a, 2), (b, 2), (c,3)}, and S = {(1, x), (2, y), (4, w)}. Determine
a. S ◦R
b. Dom(S ◦R)
c. Rng(S ◦R)
Solution
Perhaps the best way to visualize the composition of S and R is through
the arrow diagram shown in Figure 4.4. First, we draw regions to represent
the sets A, B, and C and select points in the regions to represent the elements
of the sets. Since the ordered pair (a, 1) is in the relation R, we draw an arrow
from the point a in set A to the point 1 in the set B. Since (a, 2) is in the
relation R, we draw an arrow from the point a to 2, and so forth. Once we
have completed drawing all of the arrows from A to B which represent the
relation R, we draw the arrows from points in the set B to points in the set C
which represent the relation S. To determine the set of ordered pairs in the
composition S ◦R, we select, in turn, elements in set A and ﬁnd all paths,
if any, which lead from that element to some element in C. The set of all
ordered pairs found in this manner constitutes the elements of S ◦R. For
instance, a ∈A and there is a path from a through 1 ∈B to x ∈C. Hence,
(a, x) ∈S ◦R. There is another path from a ∈A through 2 ∈B to y ∈C.
Thus, (a, y) ∈S ◦R. Using Figure 4.4 and proceeding in this fashion, we ﬁnd
S ◦R = {(a, x), (a, y), (b, y)}.
Hence, Dom(S ◦R) = {a, b} ⊂A and Rng(S ◦R) = {x, y} ⊂C.

Observe that for relations R and S as deﬁned in Example 4.1.2 the com-
position R ◦S is not deﬁned, since S is a relation from B to C and R is a
relation from A to B. Hence, the binary operation of composition, ◦, is not
commutative—that is, S ◦R ̸= R ◦S. Even if S and R are both relations on
the same set A and S ◦R and R ◦S are both deﬁned, S ◦R may not equal
R ◦S. For example, let A = {1, b}, R = {(1, b)}, and S = {(b, 1)}. Then
S ◦R = {(1, 1)}, R ◦S = {(b, b)}, and S ◦R ̸= R ◦S.
Theorem 4.1 Let R be a relation from A to B; then
a. IB ◦R = R
and
b. R ◦IA = R
Proof:
a. Assume (x, y) ∈R. Then y ∈B and (y, y) ∈IB. By the deﬁnition
of composition, since (x, y) ∈R and (y, y) ∈IB, it follows that (x, y) ∈IB ◦R.
Hence, R ⊆IB ◦R.
Now assume (x, y) ∈IB ◦R. By deﬁnition of IB ◦R there exists a b ∈B
such that (x, b) ∈R and (b, y) ∈IB. Since (b, y) ∈IB, b = y and since
(x, b) ∈R, (x, y) ∈R. Thus, IB ◦R ⊆R. Consequently, IB ◦R = R.
■
b. Proving that R ◦IA = R is an exercise.
Theorem 4.1 states that a relation R from A to B has a “left” identity—
the identity relation IB such that IB ◦R = R—and a “right” identity—the
identity relation IA such that R ◦IA = R. But unless A = B, the relation R
does not have an identity I such that I ◦R = R ◦I = R. When A = B, the
identity for the relation R on A is the relation IA.
In elementary algebra, for all real numbers x ̸= 0, there exists a real number
called the multiplicative inverse of x and denoted by x−1 with the property
that x · x−1 = x−1 · x = 1, the multiplicative identity for the set of real
numbers. Example 4.1.3 provides an example of a relation R for which neither
the composition R ◦R−1 nor the composition R−1 ◦R is an identity relation.
Example 4.1.3 Let R = {(1, 2), (1, 3), (3,2)}. Calculate R◦R−1 and R−1◦R.
Solution
The inverse relation is R−1 = {(2, 1), (3, 1), (2, 3)}.
Since (2, 1) ∈R−1
and (1, 2), (1, 3) ∈R,
(2, 2), (2, 3) ∈R ◦R−1.
Since (3, 1) ∈R−1 and
(1, 2), (1, 3) ∈R,
(3, 2), (3, 3) ∈R ◦R−1.
And since (2, 3) ∈R−1 and
(3, 2) ∈R, (2, 2) ∈R ◦R−1. Hence, R ◦R−1 = {(2, 2), (2, 3), (3,2),(3, 3)},
which is not an identity relation because (2, 3) ∈R ◦R−1.
In a like manner, we ﬁnd R−1 ◦R = {(1, 1), (1, 3), (3, 1), (3,3)}, which is
not an identity relation because (1, 3) ∈R−1 ◦R.

Let A = {1, 2},

and S = {(a, v), (b, w), (c, u)}.

(S ◦R)−1 = {(v, 1), (w, 2), (u, 2)}. Notice that R ◦S and therefore (R ◦S)−1
does not exist. We easily compute

R−1 = {(a, 1), (b, 2), (c,2)}
and

and observe that S−1 ◦R−1 does not exist; however,
R−1 ◦S−1 = {(v, 1), (w, 2), (u, 2)} = (S ◦R)−1.
This leads us to conjecture and prove Theorem 4.2.
Theorem 4.2 Let R be a relation from A to B and let S be a relation from
B to C. Then the relation from C to A, (S ◦R)−1 = R−1 ◦S−1.
Proof:
Let (c, a) ∈C × A. Then
(c, a) ∈(S ◦R)−1 ⇔(a, c) ∈S ◦R
Deﬁnition of inverse relation
⇔(∃b ∈B)[((a, b) ∈R) ∧((b, c) ∈S)]
Deﬁnition of composition
⇔(∃b ∈B)[((b, a) ∈R−1) ∧((c, b) ∈S−1)]
Deﬁnition of inverse relation
⇔(∃b ∈B)[((c, b) ∈S−1) ∧((b, a) ∈R−1)]
Commutative law of conjunction
⇔(c, a) ∈R−1 ◦S−1
Deﬁnition of composition
■
Theorem 4.2 states that the inverse of the composition of two relations,
(S ◦R)−1, is the composition of the inverse of the second relation with the
inverse of the ﬁrst relation, R−1 ◦S−1.
We proved earlier by example that the operation of composition on relations
is not commutative. That is, we showed that, in general, for relations R and
S, R ◦S ̸= S ◦R. However, the operation of composition on relations is
associative, as we state and prove in the next theorem.
Theorem 4.3 Let R be a relation from A to B, let S be a relation from B
to C, and let T be a relation from C to D. Then T ◦(S ◦R) = (T ◦S) ◦R.
Proof:
Let (a, d) ∈A × D. Then
(a, d) ∈T ◦(S ◦R)
⇔(∃c ∈C)[((a, c) ∈(S ◦R)) ∧((c, d) ∈T)]
Deﬁnition of composition
⇔(∃c ∈C)[(∃b ∈B)[((a, b) ∈R) ∧((b, c) ∈S)] ∧((c, d) ∈T)]
Deﬁnition of composition
⇔(∃c ∈C)(∃b ∈B)[((a, b) ∈R) ∧[((b, c) ∈S) ∧((c, d) ∈T)]]
Associative law of conjunction
⇔(∃b ∈B)(∃c ∈C)[((a, b) ∈R) ∧[((b, c) ∈S) ∧((c, d) ∈T)]]
A property of quantiﬁers
⇔(∃b ∈B)[((a, b) ∈R) ∧[(∃c ∈C)((b, c) ∈S) ∧((c, d) ∈T)]]
A property of quantiﬁers

⇔(∃b ∈B)[((a, b) ∈R) ∧((b, d) ∈(T ◦S))]
Deﬁnition of composition
⇔(a, d) ∈(T ◦S) ◦R

4.2
The Order Relations
<,
≤,
>,
≥
In addition to the operations of addition, +, and multiplication, ·, the sets of
natural numbers, integers, rational numbers, and real numbers have an order
relation < (read “less than”) which satisﬁes certain axioms. These axioms
can be expressed in terms of the undeﬁned concept of positiveness. A set
of numbers S is ordered if there exists a subset of positive numbers Sp
which satisfy the axioms:
O1. (Trichotomy Law)
For all x ∈S exactly one of the following three
statements is true:
x = 0,
x ∈Sp,
−x ∈Sp.
O2. For all x, y ∈Sp,
x + y ∈Sp.
O3. For all x, y ∈Sp,
x · y ∈Sp.

Relations
169
We deﬁne negative, <, ≤, >, ≥
as follows:
The number x is negative if and only if −x is positive.
The order relation < (read “is less than”) is deﬁned by x < y ⇔y −x is
positive.
The order relation > (read “is greater than”) is deﬁned by x > y ⇔x−y
is positive.
The order relation ≤(read “is less than or equal to”) is deﬁned by
x ≤y ⇔x < y or x = y.
The order relation ≥(read “is greater than or equal to”) is deﬁned by
x ≥y ⇔x > y or x = y.
Observe that x < y and y > x are equivalent statements as are x ≤y
and y ≥x.
Likewise, the conjunction x ≤y and y ≤z can be abbre-
viated by the single statement x ≤y ≤z.
Similarly, x > y > z ⇔
x > y and y > z, and x ≥y ≥z ⇔x ≥y and y ≥z. The natural numbers,
the integers, the rational numbers, and the real numbers with the usual op-
erations of addition, +, and multiplication, ·, and the order relation, <, are
all ordered sets. The integers are an example of an integral domain and the
rational numbers and the real numbers are examples of ordered ﬁelds.
Using the axioms and deﬁnitions stated above, for w, x, y, z ∈R the follow-
ing theorems can be proved.
T1. 0 < x ⇔x is positive.
T2. The transitive law holds for <: If x < y and y < z, then x < z.
T3. The trichotomy law holds for <: If x, y ∈R exactly one of the follow-
ing statements is true: x = y,
x < y,
y < x.
T4. x < y ⇒x + z < y + z.
T5. x < 0 ⇔x is negative.
T6. If x < 0 and y < 0, then x+y < 0. (The sum of two negative numbers
is a negative number.)
T7. If x < 0 and y < 0, then x · y > 0. (The product of two negative
numbers is a positive number.)
T8. If x ̸= 0, then 0 < x2.
T9. 1 > 0.
T10. If 0 < x < y, then 0 < 1
y < 1
x.
T11. If x < y and 0 < z, then xz < yz.
T12. If x < y and z < 0, then yz < xz.
T13. If x < y, then −y < −x.
T14. If x < y and z < w, then x + z < y + w.
T15. If 0 < x < y and 0 < z < w, then
xz < yw
and
x
w < y
z .
T16. If x ≤y and y ≤z, then x ≤z.
T17. If x ≤y and y ≤x, then x = y.

170
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
T18. If x ≤y and 0 < z, then xz ≤yz.
T19. If x ≤y and z < 0, then yz ≤xz.
Let A be a nonempty set of real numbers. The number m ∈A is the least
element of A (smallest element of A or minimum of A) if and only if
for every x ∈A, m ≤x. The number M ∈A is the greatest element of A
(largest element of A or maximum of A) if and only if for every x ∈A,
M ≥x.
Some nonempty subsets of R have least elements; others do not. For ex-
ample, the set of natural numbers N has a least element which is 1. The set
of integers Z does not have a least element. The open interval (−2, 3) does
not have a least element. Although for all x ∈(−2, 3), −2 ≤x, −2 is not
a least element of (−2, 3), because −2 /∈(−2, 3). On the other hand, −2 is
the least element of the interval [−2, 3), because −2 ≤x for all x ∈[−2, 3)
and −2 ∈[−2, 3). The following theorem proves if a set of real numbers has
a least element, it is unique.
Theorem 4.4 The least element of a set A of real numbers is unique.
Proof:
Suppose x and y are distinct least elements of A. Since x is a least
element of A and y ∈A,
x ≤y. Also, since y is a least element of A and
x ∈A, y ≤x. By Theorem T17, x = y, and the least element of A is unique.
■
The Archimedean Property of Euclidean geometry states that any length
no matter how large can be exceeded by repeatedly “marking oﬀ” a given
length no matter how small. The following is the algebraic formulation of the
Archimedean Property for the set of natural numbers.
Theorem 4.5 The Archimedean Property for the Natural Numbers
For all m, n ∈N there exists a k ∈N such that m < kn.
Proof:
By T9, 0 < 1. Adding m and using T4, we ﬁnd that
m = 0 + m < 1 + m = m + 1.
That is, (1) m < m + 1. Since 1 is the least element of N and since 0 < 1,
(2) 0 < 1 ≤m and (3) 1 ≤n. From (1) and (2), we have (4) 0 < m < m+1.
Multiplying (3) by m + 1 > 0 yields (5) 1 · (m + 1) ≤n · (m + 1) by T18. It
follows from (4) and (5) that m < m + 1 ≤(m + 1) · n. That is, the value
k = m + 1 ∈N makes the conclusion of the Archimedean Property true.
■
A nonempty set A of real numbers is well-ordered if and only if every
nonempty subset of A has a least element. For example, the nonempty set
A = {−1, π, e} is well-ordered, because each of the nonempty subsets {−1},
{π}, {e}, {−1, π}, {−1, e}, {π, e}, and A all have a least element. It is fairly
evident from this example that any nonempty, ﬁnite subset of real numbers
is well-ordered. The open interval (−2, 3) is not well-ordered, since it has
no least element. Although the interval [−2, 3) has a least element, it is not
well-ordered since the subset (−2, 3) has no least element. The sets Z, Q, and

Relations
171
R are not well-ordered, because none of these sets has a least element. Even
though it appears obvious that the set of natural numbers N is well-ordered,
a proof of this fact requires the use of the Axiom of Induction, which we will
not study until Chapter 6. Thus, for now, we state the well-ordering principle
without proof.
The Well-Ordering Principle:
The set of natural numbers is well-
ordered by the relation <.
It follows easily from the well-ordering principle that the set of whole num-
bers W = N ∪{0} is well-ordered by <, since any nonempty set of W which
contains the element 0 has 0 as its least element and since any nonempty set
of W which does not contain 0 is a nonempty subset of N and therefore has
a least element by the well-ordering principle.
In elementary school, you learned how to use a process called “long division”
to divide one natural number b, called the dividend, by a natural number a,
called the divisor, to obtain two whole numbers q, called the quotient, and
r, called the remainder. The whole numbers q and r are unique and satisfy
the equation b = aq + r where 0 ≤r < a. We now state and prove a theorem
called the division algorithm for natural numbers.
Theorem 4.6 The Division Algorithm for the Natural Numbers
For all a, b ∈N there exists unique q, r ∈W such that b = aq + r and
0 ≤r < a.
Proof:
Let a, b ∈N. Consider the set S = {b−ax | x ∈W and b−ax ≥0}.
Since x = 0 ∈W and b ∈N, b −a · 0 = b ≥0. Thus, by deﬁnition of S,
b ∈S and S is a nonempty subset of the whole numbers. As a consequence
of the well-ordering principle, S has a smallest element r and by deﬁnition of
S, r ≥0. Since r ∈S, there exists a q ∈W such that r = b −aq. That is,
there exist whole numbers q and r such that b = aq + r and r ≥0.
We now show that r < a by contradiction. Assume to the contrary a ≤r
and let d = r −a ≥0. Since a ∈N, a > 0 and d < r. Furthermore,
d = r −a = (b −aq) −a = b −a(q + 1) ≥0.
Consequently, d ∈S and d < r, which contradicts r being the smallest element
of S. Thus, we have 0 ≤r < a.
In order to show that the whole numbers q and r which satisfy b = aq + r
and 0 ≤r < a are unique, assume there exists a second pair of whole numbers
q2 ̸= q and r2 ̸= r such that b = aq2 + r2 and 0 ≤r2 < a. Since b = aq + r =
aq2+r2, (1) a(q−q2) = r2−r. By deﬁnition a divides r2−r, because a, q−q2,
and r2 −r are integers. Since 0 ≤r < a, −a < −r ≤0. Adding 0 ≤r2 < a
to this last inequality, we obtain −a < r2 −r < a. Assume r2 ≥r then
(2) 0 ≤r2 −r < a. Remark: Instead of assuming r2 ≥r, we could assume
r ≥r2 and reach the same conclusions throughout the remainder of the proof
by interchanging r and r2. Since a divides the nonnegative integer r2 −r and
since from (2) 0·a ≤r2−r < 1·a, either there is an integer between 0 and 1 or

172
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
r2 = r. Because there is no integer between 0 and 1, (3) r2 = r. Substituting
(3) into (1), we ﬁnd a(q −q2) = 0, which implies a = 0 or q −q2 = 0. Since
a ∈N, a ̸= 0 and therefore q = q2.
■
The concept of the greatest lower bound of a set is similar to, but not the
same as, the concept of the least element, and the concept of a least upper
bound of a set is similar to the concept of a greatest element. We deﬁne these
new concepts below.
Let A be a subset of the real numbers R. The number m ∈R is a lower
bound for A if m ≤x for all x ∈A.
The number m ∈R is the greatest lower bound for A (inﬁmum of A)
(i) if m is a lower bound for A and (ii) if ℓis a lower bound for A, then m ≥ℓ.
The number u ∈R is an upper bound for A if u ≥x for all x ∈A.
The number u ∈R is the least upper bound for A (supremum of A)
(i) if u is an upper bound for A and (ii) if t is an upper bound for A, then
u ≤t.
The set A is bounded if it has a lower bound and an upper bound.
The set A1 = {−3, −1, 3, 7} has lower bounds of −20, −8, −5 and −3. A1
has a greatest lower bound of −3, and the least element of A1 is −3. A1 has
upper bounds of 7, 11, 15, and 24. A1 has a least upper bound of 7, and the
greatest element of A1 is 7. The set A1 is bounded. The set A2 = (5, 11] has
lower bounds of −2, 0, and 5. The greatest lower bound of A2 is 5, but A2
has no least element since 5 /∈A2. A2 has upper bounds of 13, 17, and 24.
The least upper bound of A2 is 11, and the greatest element of A2 is 11. A2
is bounded. The set A3 = (−∞, 4) is not bounded below and therefore has
no least element. A3 is bounded above, has a least upper bound of 4, but has
no greatest element.
A ﬁeld F with addition + and multiplication · which is ordered by the
relation < is a complete ordered ﬁeld if and only if every nonempty subset
of F which has an upper bound has a least upper bound in F.
Both the rational number system and the real number system are ordered
ﬁelds. The real number system is a complete ordered ﬁeld, but the rational
number system is not a complete ordered ﬁeld. This is one distinction between
the ﬁeld of rational numbers and the ﬁeld of real numbers. We will not prove
the ordered ﬁeld of real numbers is complete; however, after we prove the
Archimedean Property of the real numbers we will prove the ordered ﬁeld of
rational numbers is not complete.
Theorem 4.7 The Archimedean Property for the Real Numbers
If x, y ∈R,
x > 0, and
y > 0, then there exists an n ∈N such that
nx > y.
Proof: If this theorem were false, the inequality nx ≤y would hold for all
n ∈N. That is, the set A = {nx | n ∈N, x ∈R and x > 0} would be
bounded above by y. The set A is nonempty, since x ∈A. Since the ﬁeld

Relations
173
of real numbers is complete, there exists a u ∈R which is the least upper
bound for A. Thus, nx ≤u for all n ∈N. Hence, (n + 1)x ≤u for all n ∈N.
Consequently, u−x is an upper bound for A. But u−x < u, which contradicts
u being the least upper bound for A.
■
Corollary 4.7.1 If y ∈R, there exists an n ∈N such that n > y.
Proof: If y ≤0, let n = 1. If y > 0, let x = 1 in Theorem 4.7.

n < p.

p > 0. Then by Theorem 4.7 there exists an
n ∈N such that n · 1 > 1
p or equivalently 1
n < p.

The following theorem states that for all real numbers x there is a unique
integer n such that x ∈[n, n + 1).
Theorem 4.9 If x ∈R, there exists a unique n ∈Z such that n ≤x < n+1.
Proof: Case 1. If x is an integer, let n = x and the conclusion is true.
Case 2. If x is not an integer, by Corollary 4.7.1 there exists a natural
number k such that k > x.
Also by Corollary 4.7.1 there exists a natu-
ral number ℓsuch that ℓ> −x, which implies −ℓ< x.
That is, there
are two integers k and m = −ℓsuch that m < x < k.
Since x is not
an integer, x must lie between two consecutive integers in the ﬁnite set
S = {m, m + 1, m + 2, . . ., k}.
Suppose n1 and n2 are two distinct inte-
gers in S such that n1 < x < n1 + 1 and n2 < x < n2 + 1. Assume n1 < n2.

Then n1 < n2 < x < n1 +1. That is, there is an integer (namely, n2) between
n1 and n1 + 1. Contradiction.

An important property of both the set of rational numbers and the set of
irrational numbers is that they are both dense in the set of real numbers.
That is, between any two distinct real numbers there is a rational number
and an irrational number.
As a matter of fact, between any two distinct
real numbers there are an inﬁnite number of rational numbers and an inﬁnite
number of irrational numbers. In the following theorem, we prove the set of
rational numbers is dense in the set of real numbers.
Theorem 4.10 If a, b ∈R and a < b, then there exists a rational number r
such that a < r < b.
Proof: Since a < b, b −a > 0 and by Corollary 4.7.2 there exists a natural

Solving the right-hand inequality of (2) for a yields (4) a < m
n . Combining
(1), (3), and (4), we have a < m
n ≤a + 1
n < b. Observe that r1 = m
n is a
rational number such that a < r1 < b.

In the proof of Theorem 4.10 the rational number r1 satisﬁes r1 < b. So by

r2 < b, and a third rational number r3 which satisﬁes a < r1 < r2 < r3 < b,
etc. Thus, there are an inﬁnite number of rational numbers between a and b.
We now show that between every two distinct rational numbers there is an

t such that r < t < s.

4.3
Reﬂexive, Symmetric, Transitive, and Equivalence
Relations
Often relations are classiﬁed based upon one or more properties they satisfy.
Usually, relations with the same classiﬁcations have additional like properties
as well. Three important properties used to classify relations are the proper-
ties of being reﬂexive, symmetric, or transitive.
Let R be a relation on a set A. R is a reﬂexive relation on A if and only
if (∀x ∈A)[(x, x) ∈R].
R is a symmetric relation on A if and only if
(∀x, y ∈A)[((x, y) ∈R) ⇒((y, x) ∈R)].
R is a transitive relation on A if and only if
(∀x, y, z ∈A)[(((x, y) ∈R) ∧((y, z) ∈R)) ⇒((x, z) ∈R)].
A relation R is an equivalence relation on A if and only if R is reﬂexive,
symmetric, and transitive on A.
For a nonempty set A, the reﬂexive property is the only property which re-
quires some ordered pair to be in the relation R. The symmetric and transitive
properties merely specify conditions which the ordered pairs in the relation
must satisfy to be symmetric or transitive. Since the identity relation on A is
IA = {(x, x) | x ∈A}, a relation R is reﬂexive on A if and only if IA ⊆R.
Negating the above deﬁnitions, we ﬁnd
A relation R on a set A is not reﬂexive ⇔(∃x ∈A)((x, x) /∈R).
A relation R on a set A is not symmetric ⇔
(∃x, y ∈A)[((x, y) ∈R) ∧((y, x) /∈R)].
A relation R on a set A is not transitive ⇔
(∃x, y, z ∈A)[((x, y) ∈R) ∧((y, z) ∈R) ∧((x, z) /∈R)].
Thus, to show a relation R is not reﬂexive, we must ﬁnd a single x ∈A such
that (x, x) /∈R. To show that R is not symmetric, we must ﬁnd an x and y in
A such that (x, y) ∈R and (y, x) /∈R. To show R is not transitive, we must
ﬁnd some x, y, and z (not necessarily distinct) in A such that (x, y) ∈R,
(y, z) ∈R, and (x, z) /∈R.

Relations
177
Example 4.3.1 Consider the relation S = {(1, 1), (2, 2), (3, 3),(1, 2), (2,3)}
on the set A = {1, 2, 3}.
Reﬂexive:
S is reﬂexive on A, since IA = {(1, 1), (2, 2), (3,3)} ⊂S.
Symmetric: S is not symmetric on A, since 1, 2 ∈A, (1, 2) ∈S, but (2, 1) /∈S.
Transitive: S is not transitive on A, since 1, 2, 3 ∈A, (1, 2) ∈S and (2, 3) ∈S,
but (1, 3) /∈S.
The relation S is reﬂexive, is not symmetric, and is not transitive on set A.
■
Example 4.3.2 The set T = {(1, 2), (2, 1)} is a relation on the set B = {1, 2}.
Reﬂexive:
T is not reﬂexive on B, since 1 ∈B but (1, 1) /∈T.
Symmetric: T is symmetric on B, because (1, 2) ∈T and (2, 1) ∈T and
because there are no other ordered pairs in T.
Transitive: T is not transitive on B, since 1, 2 ∈B, (1, 2) ∈T and (2, 1) ∈T,
but (1, 1) /∈T.
The relation T is not reﬂexive, is symmetric, and is not transitive on set B.
■
Example 4.3.3 Let A = {1, 2, 3} and V = {(1, 2), (1, 3), (2,3)}.
Reﬂexive:
V is not reﬂexive on A, since 1 ∈A but (1, 1) /∈V .
Symmetric: V is not symmetric on A, since 1, 2 ∈A and (1, 2) ∈V , but
(2, 1) /∈V .
Transitive: V is transitive on A, since (1, 2) ∈V , (2, 3) ∈V and (1, 3) ∈V ,
and there are no two other ordered pairs in V of the form (a, b), (b, c).
The relation V is not reﬂexive, is not symmetric, but is transitive on set A.
■
Example 4.3.4
Let W be the relation W = {(1, 1), (2, 2)} on the set B =
{1, 2}.
Reﬂexive:
W is reﬂexive on B, since IB = W.
Symmetric: W is symmetric since (1, 1) ∈W implies (1, 1) ∈W and
(2, 2) ∈W implies (2, 2) ∈W.
Transitive: The hypothesis of the implication [((x, y) ∈W)∧((y, z) ∈W)] ⇒
((x, z) ∈W) is false for the relation W. Hence, the implication is true, and
W is transitive vacuously.
Since the relation W is reﬂexive, symmetric, and transitive on set B, W is an
equivalence relation on set B.
■
The empty relation ∅, which has no ordered pairs, is another example of an
equivalence relation. That is, the empty relation is reﬂexive, symmetric, and
transitive.

178
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
Of course, relations do not need to be speciﬁed as a set of ordered pairs.
When a relation is speciﬁed in another manner, logical arguments must be
used in determining whether the relation is reﬂexive, symmetric, or transitive.
Example 4.3.5 Let L be the set of all lines in the Euclidean plane and let
̸⊥be the relation “is not perpendicular to.”
Reﬂexive:
Let ℓ∈L. The statement “ℓ̸⊥ℓ” is true, so the relation ̸⊥is
reﬂexive.
Symmetric: Let ℓ, m ∈L. The statement “If ℓ̸⊥m, then m ̸⊥ℓ” is true, so
̸⊥is symmetric.
Transitive: Let ℓ, m, n ∈L. The statement “If ℓ̸⊥m and m ̸⊥n, then ℓ̸⊥n”
is false, since ℓcould be perpendicular to n and the line m could be any line
other than ℓor n which passes through the point of intersection of the lines ℓ
and n. Thus, ̸⊥is not transitive.
Consequently, the relation ̸⊥is reﬂexive and symmetric, but not transitive on
set L.
■
Example 4.3.6 Consider the relation “is a multiple of” on the set of natural
numbers.
Reﬂexive:
For all n ∈N, n = 1 · n, so the statement “n is a multiple of n”
is true. Thus, “is a multiple of” is a reﬂexive relation on N.
Symmetric: Let m, n ∈N. The statement “If m is a multiple of n, then n
is a multiple of m” is false. A counterexample is m = 6 and n = 2, because
m = 3·n, but there is no natural number k such that n = k·m—that is, there
is no natural number k such that 2 = k · 6. Hence, the relation “is a multiple
of” is not symmetric on N.
Transitive:
Let m, n, p ∈N. “If m is a multiple of n and n is a multiple of
p, then m is a multiple of p” is a true statement. This is a theorem which we
proved earlier.
The relation “is a multiple of” is reﬂexive and transitive, but not symmetric
on N.
■
Example 4.3.7 The set Y = {(2, 2), (2, 3), (3,2),(3, 3)} is a relation on the
set C = {1, 2, 3}.
Reﬂexive:
Y is not reﬂexive on C, since 1 ∈C but (1, 1) /∈Y .
Symmetric: Y is symmetric on C, because (2, 2) ∈Y and (2, 2) ∈Y , because
(2, 3) ∈Y and (3, 2) ∈Y , and because (3, 3) ∈Y and (3, 3) ∈Y .
Transitive: Y is transitive on C, since for all combinations of ordered pairs
of the form (a, b) ∈Y and (b, c) ∈Y the ordered pair (a, c) ∈Y . There
are eight combinations to verify. For instance, (2, 2) ∈Y , (2, 2) ∈Y , and
(2, 2) ∈Y . Also, (2, 2) ∈Y , (2, 3) ∈Y , and (2, 3) ∈Y . Verify the remaining
six combinations.
The relation Y is not reﬂexive, but is symmetric and transitive on set C.
■

Relations
179
Example 4.3.8 Let P be the set of all living people and consider the relation
“is the mother of.”
Reﬂexive:
For a ∈P, “a is the mother of a” is a false statement. Hence, the
relation “is the mother of” is not reﬂexive on set P.
Symmetric: Let a, b ∈P. The statement “If a is the mother of b, then b is the
mother of a” is false. Thus, the relation “is the mother of” is not symmetric
on set P.
Transitive: Let a, b, c ∈P. The statement “If a is the mother of b and b is the
mother of c, then a is the mother of c” is false, since a is the maternal grand-
mother of c. Consequently, the relation “is the mother of” is not transitive
on set P.
The relation “is the mother of” is not reﬂexive, is not symmetric, and is not
transitive on set P.
■
We have deﬁned three properties for relations—the reﬂexive property, the
symmetric property, and the transitive property. Given a relation, it is reﬂex-
ive or not, it is symmetric or not, and it is transitive or not. Consequently, a
relation may have any one of eight diﬀerent combinations of these properties.
So far, we have presented eight relations—one which has each of the possible
combinations of properties. Displayed in Table 4.1 is each type of relation
and an example which corresponds to that type. In the table, the symbol T
indicates the relation has the speciﬁed property, while the symbol F indicates
it does not.

Example 4.3.9 Let R be the relation deﬁned on the set of integers, Z, by
(a, b) ∈R ⇔|a| = |b|. Prove R is an equivalence relation on Z.
Proof: Let a ∈Z. Since |a| = |a|, (a, a) ∈R and R is reﬂexive on Z.
Suppose (a, b) ∈R. Then by deﬁnition of R, |a| = |b|. Since equality is a
reﬂexive relation, |b| = |a|. Hence, (b, a) ∈R and R is symmetric on Z.

180
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
Suppose (a, b) ∈R and (b, c) ∈R. Then by deﬁnition |a| = |b| and |b| = |c|.
Since equality is a transitive relation, |a| = |c|, (a, c) ∈R, and R is transitive
on Z.
Since R is reﬂexive, symmetric, and transitive, R is an equivalence relation
on Z.
■
In previous examples, the elements of the set A were usually numbers, lines,
or people. That is, the elements of A were singular entities. In the following
example, the elements of A are ordered pairs. In general, the elements of A
may be almost anything.
Example 4.3.10 Let A = Z×Z, the Cartesian product of the set of integers.
Deﬁne the relation P on Z × Z by ((a, b), (c, d)) ∈P ⇔ab = cd. Prove P is
an equivalence relation on A.
Proof: For all a, b ∈Z, ab = ab. Consequently, for a, b ∈Z, ((a, b), (a, b)) ∈P
and P is reﬂexive.
Suppose ((a, b), (c, d)) ∈P. Then by deﬁnition of P, ab = cd. Since equality
is a reﬂexive relation, cd = ab. Hence, ((c, d), (a, b)) ∈P and P is symmetric.
Suppose ((a, b), (c, d)) ∈P and ((c, d), (e, f)) ∈P. Then by deﬁnition of
P, ab = cd and cd = ef. Since equality is a transitive relation, ab = ef, and
((a, b), (e, f)) ∈P. Consequently, P is transitive.
Since P is reﬂexive, symmetric, and transitive, P is an equivalence relation
on Z × Z.
■
We now prove that the inverse relation of an equivalence relation is also an
equivalence relation.
Theorem 4.13 If E is an equivalence relation on the set A, then E−1 is an
equivalence relation on A.
Proof: Let a ∈A. Since E is an equivalence relation on A, (a, a) ∈E.
By deﬁnition of the inverse relation of E, (a, a) ∈E−1 also. Hence, E−1 is
reﬂexive on A.
Suppose (a, b) ∈E−1. Then (b, a) ∈E. Since E is an equivalence relation
on A, E is symmetric and (a, b) ∈E. Therefore, (b, a) ∈E−1 and E−1 is
symmetric on A.
Finally, suppose (a, b) ∈E−1 and (b, c) ∈E−1.
From the deﬁnition of
inverse relation it follows that (b, a) ∈E and (c, b) ∈E.
Since E is an
equivalence relation, E is transitive and (c, a) ∈E. Consequently, (a, c) ∈E−1
and E−1 is transitive on A.
Since E−1 is reﬂexive, symmetric, and transitive, E−1 is an equivalence
relation on A.

1. Let A = {a, b}. For each relation deﬁned below, determine whether the
relation is reﬂexive, symmetric, or transitive on A.
a. R1 = {(b, a)}
b. R2 = {(b, a), (a, b)}
c. R3 = {(b, a), (a, b), (a, a)}
d. R4 = {(b, a), (a, b), (a, a), (b,b)}
e. R5 = {(b, b), (a, a)}
2. Which relations in Exercise 1 are equivalence relations?
3. Let A = {1, 2, 3, 4, 5}. Consider the following relations on A.
T1 = {(x, y) | x < y}
T2 = {(x, y) | x ≤y}
T3 = {(x, y) | x = y}
T4 = {(x, y) | x is a factor of y}
T5 = {(x, y) | x is a prime factor of y}
For each relation Ti, i = 1, 2, . . ., 5
a. Determine Dom(Ti) and Rng(Ti).
b. Graph Ti on A × A.
c. Write Ti as a set of ordered pairs.
d. Determine if Ti is reﬂexive, symmetric, or transitive.
e. Determine which Ti are equivalence relations on the set A.
4. Let A be the set of all living people. Consider the following relations
on A.
U1 = {(x, y) | x lives on the same street as y}
U2 = {(x, y) | x lives next door to y}
U3 = {(x, y) | x is a descendant of y}
a. Determine whether U1, U2, and U3 are reﬂexive, symmetric, or tran-
sitive on A.
b. Which relations are equivalence relations?
5. Let A = R −{0} and P = {(x, y) | xy > 0}. Prove P is an equivalence
relation on A.
6. Let A = R and Q = {(x, y) | xy ≥0}. Is Q is an equivalence relation
on R? Justify your answer.
7. Prove R = {(x, y) | x + y is an even integer} is an equivalence relation
on Z.
8. Is S = {(x, y) | x + y is an odd integer} an equivalence relation on Z?
Justify your answer.

c. If R and S are transitive on A, then R ∩S is transitive on A.

a. If R is an equivalence relation on A, then R ◦R is an equivalence
relation on A.
b. If R and S are equivalence relations on A, then R ◦S is an equi-
valence relation on A.
4.4
Equivalence Relations, Equivalence Classes, and Par-
titions
It is easy to verify that the relation R = {(1, 1), (1, 2), (2,1),(2, 2),(3,3),
(4, 4), (4, 5), (5, 4),(4, 6), (6,4), (5,5),(5, 6), (6,5), (6,6)} is an equivalence re-
lation on the set A = {1, 2, 3, 4, 5,6}. Observe that the element 1 ∈A is
R-related to the elements 1 and 2, since (1, 1) ∈R, (1, 2) ∈R, and (2, 1) ∈R.
Likewise, the element 2 is R-related to 1 and 2. The element 3 is R-related to
3, since (3, 3) ∈R. Because (4, 4), (4, 5), (5, 4), (4, 6), (6, 4) ∈R, the element
4 is R-related to 4, 5, and 6. We also ﬁnd 5 is R-related to 4, 5, and 6 and 6
is R-related to 4, 5, and 6. Thus, associated with each element x ∈A there

Relations

is a set Sx which consists of all elements of A that are R-related to x. The
following table summarizes our ﬁndings for the given relation R on the given
set A.

Sx
1
{1, 2}
2
{1, 2}

{4, 5, 6}

Observe that S1 = S2, S4 = S5 = S6 and that A = S1 ∪S3 ∪S4. Assuming
A ̸= ∅, for any x ∈A the set Sx of elements which are R-related to x is
nonempty, because R is reﬂexive on A and therefore (x, x) ∈R. That is, if
A ̸= ∅and x ∈A, then x ∈Sx. Also notice that the equivalence relation R
subdivides the set A into subsets of R-equivalent elements. We formalize our
ﬁndings with the following two deﬁnitions.
Let R be an equivalence relation on the nonempty set A. For x ∈A, the
equivalence class of x determined by the relation R is the set
[x]R = {y ∈A | (x, y) ∈R}.
The set of all equivalence classes is called A modulo R (or A mod R) and
is denoted by
A/R = {[x]R | x ∈A}.
The mathematical expression [x]R = {y ∈A | (x, y) ∈R} which deﬁnes the
equivalence class of x modulo R is read “the equivalence class of x modulo
R is the set of all y in A such that (x, y) is in R.”
Any element y ∈A
which appears with x in any ordered pair of R is in the set [x]R.
When
it is obvious what relation R we are discussing, we will omit the subscript
R from the equivalence class notation—that is, we will abbreviate [x]R as
[x].
In the example above, the equivalence classes are [1] = [2] = {1, 2},
[3] = {3}, and [4] = [5] = [6] = {4, 5, 6}; and the set of all equivalence classes
is A/R = {{1, 2}, {3},{4,5,6}}.
Let A be a nonempty set and let A be a collection of subsets of A. The
collection of sets A is a partition of A if and only if
(1) X ∈A ⇒X ̸= ∅
(2) X ∈A and Y ∈A ⇒either X = Y or X ∩Y = ∅
(3) S
X∈A X = A
On a speciﬁed date at a particular university, the set of undergraduate
students is partitioned (1) by class standing (freshman, sophomore, junior,
senior), (2) by major, (3) by age, (4) by sex, (5) by residency, etc. The United
States is partitioned geographically in several diﬀerent ways. It is partitioned
into states, into time zones, into zip code areas by the postal service, and into
area codes by the telephone companies.

In the example we have been studying, the set A = {1, 2, 3, 4,5, 6} is parti-
tioned by the relation R into the collection of sets
A = A/R = {{1, 2}, {3}, {4,5, 6}}.
In this section, we will study properties of equivalence relations on a set A,
properties of partitions of a set A, and the connection between equivalence
relations and partitions.
First, we prove the following theorem regarding
equivalence classes.
Theorem 4.14 Let R be an equivalence relation on a nonempty set A and
let a, b ∈A. If b ∈[a]R, then [b]R = [a]R.
Proof:
Assume b ∈[a]R. By the deﬁnition of an equivalence class (a, b) ∈R.
To show that [b]R = [a]R, we show [b]R ⊆[a]R and [a]R ⊆[b]R. Let x ∈[b]R.
By deﬁnition (b, x) ∈R. Since (a, b) ∈R and R is transitive on A, (a, x) ∈R
and, therefore, x ∈[a]R. Thus, [b]R ⊆[a]R. Now let y ∈[a]R. By deﬁnition
(a, y) ∈R. Since (a, b) ∈R and R is symmetric on A, (b, a) ∈R. Because
(b, a) ∈R, (a, y) ∈R, and R is transitive, (b, y) ∈R. Consequently, y ∈[b]R.
Hence, [a]R ⊆[b]R. Since [a]R ⊆[b]R and [b]R ⊆[a]R, [b]R = [a]R.
■
We state the following theorems for equivalence classes and leave them for
you to prove as exercises.
Theorem 4.15 Let R be an equivalence relation on a nonempty set A and
let a, b ∈A. Then [a]R = [b]R ⇔(a, b) ∈R.
Theorem 4.16 Let R be an equivalence relation on a nonempty set A and
let a, b ∈A. If [a]R ∩[b]R ̸= ∅, then [a]R = [b]R.
Theorem 4.17 Let R be an equivalence relation on a nonempty set A and
let a, b ∈A. If [a]R ∩[b]R = ∅⇔(a, b) /∈R.
Theorem 4.18 Let R be an equivalence relation on a nonempty set A and
let a, b, c, d ∈A. If c ∈[a]R, if d ∈[b]R, and if [a]R ̸= [b]R, then (c, d) /∈R.
The following theorem states that an equivalence relation R on a nonempty
set A produces a partition of A.
Theorem 4.19 Let R be an equivalence relation on a nonempty set A. Then
A/R is a partition of A.
Proof:
To show that the collection of all equivalence classes
A/R = {[x]R | x ∈A}
is a partition of A, we must show three things:
(1) [x]R ∈A/R ⇒[x]R ̸= ∅
(2) [x]R ∈A/R and [y]R ∈A/R ⇒either [x]R = [y]R or [x]R ∩[y]R = ∅
(3) S
x∈A[x]R = A
(1) For each x ∈A, x ∈[x]R by deﬁnition. Hence, each equivalence class
[x]R in A/R is nonempty.

Relations
185
(2) For [x]R ∈A/R and [y]R ∈A/R either (i) [x]R ∩[y]R ̸= ∅or
(ii) [x]R ∩[y]R = ∅. In case (i) [x]R ∩[y]R ̸= ∅⇔(x, y) ∈R by the con-
trapositive of Theorem 4.17. By Theorem 4.15, (x, y) ∈R ⇔[x]R = [y]R.
Hence, [x]R ∩[y]R ̸= ∅⇔[x]R = [y]R.
(3) Consider S
x∈A[x]R. Since [x]R is a subset of A, S
x∈A[x]R ⊆A. Let y
be any element of A. Since y ∈[y]R, y ∈S
x∈A[x]R. Thus, A ⊆S
x∈A[x]R
and therefore A = S
x∈A[x]R.
Consequently, the collection of equivalence classes A/R (A modulo R) is a
partition of the set A.

Example 4.4.1
Let d be a positive integer and let ≡d be the relation
deﬁned on the set of integers, Z, by
m ≡d n ⇔there exists a k ∈Z such that m −n = kd.
The expression m ≡d n is read “m is congruent to n modulo d” or simply
“m is congruent to n mod d.” Sometimes this relation is written in the form
m ≡n(mod d) which is also read as stated above. An equivalent way to deﬁne
this relation is
m ≡d n ⇔m −n is divisible by d.
a. Prove congruence modulo d is an equivalence relation on Z.
b. Write the distinct equivalence classes of the equivalence relation m ≡4 n.
c. Write the partition Z/ ≡4.
Solution
a. Let d be a positive integer. Let m ∈Z. Since m −m = 0 = 0 · d, the
relation ≡d is reﬂexive.
Let m, n ∈Z and suppose m ≡d n. Then there exists a k ∈Z such that
m −n = k · d. Hence, n −m = (−k) · d. Since −k ∈Z, n ≡d m and the
relation ≡d is symmetric.
Let m, n, p ∈Z and suppose m ≡d n and n ≡d p. Then there exist
integers k1 and k2 such that m −n = k1 · d and n −p = k2 · d. Adding
these two equations, we ﬁnd m −p = k1 · d + k2 · d = (k1 + k2) · d. Since
Z is closed under addition, m ≡d p. That is, ≡d is transitive.
Since the relation ≡d is reﬂexive, symmetric, and transitive on Z, ≡d is
an equivalence relation on Z.
b. For d = 4, the distinct equivalence classes for the equivalence relation
≡4 are
[0] = {. . ., −12, −8, −4, 0, 4,8,12,. ..}
[1] = {. . ., −11, −7, −3, 1, 5,9,13,. ..}
[2] = {. . ., −10, −6, −2, 2, 6,10,14,. ..}
[3] = {. . ., −9, −5, −1, 3, 7,11,15,. ..}
c. The partition of Z corresponding to the equivalence relation ≡4 is
Z/ ≡4 = {[0], [1], [2],[3]}. ■

The collection A = {{a, c}, {b, d},{e}} is a partition of the set A = {a, b, c,
d, e} because (1) each set in A is nonempty, (2) if X, Y ∈A, then either
X = Y or X ∩Y = ∅, and (3)
S
X∈A X = A.
How do we obtain the
relation R on the set A which corresponds to the partition A? Consider the
set {a, c} ∈A. For {a, c} to be an equivalence class of R, a must be R-related
to a, a must be R-related to c, c must be R-related to a, and c must be R-
related to c. That is, it must be the case that (a, a) ∈R, (a, c) ∈R, (c, a) ∈R,
and (c, c) ∈R. If we let A1 = {a, c}, we see we must have A1 × A1 ⊆R.
Likewise, we must have {b, d} × {b, d} ⊆R, and {e} × {e} ⊆R. Hence,
R = {(a, a), (a, c), (c, a), (c, c), (b,b),(b, d), (d,b), (d, d), (e, e)}.
Consequently, for a partition A which is a ﬁnite collection of ﬁnite sets, we
have a relatively simple technique for constructing an equivalence relation R
corresponding to the partition A. For every set A ∈A we form the Cartesian
product A × A.
The union of these sets of ordered pairs is the required
equivalence relation R.
Let A = R × R, the Cartesian plane, and for r ∈[0, ∞) deﬁne Cr =
{(x, y) | x2 + y2 = r2}. The collection C = {Cr | r ∈[0, ∞)} is a partition of
the plane. For r = 0, C0 = {(0, 0)}. That is, C0 contains only the origin of
the plane. For r > 0, Cr is the circle with center at the origin and radius r.
The collection C is a partition of the plane, because
(1) no Cr is empty (for any r ∈[0, ∞), (0, r) ∈Cr),
(2) Cr, Cs ∈C ⇒either Cr = Cs (which occurs when r = s) or Cr ∩Cs = ∅
(which occurs when r ̸= s),
(3) S
r∈[0,∞) Cr = R × R (for any (a, b) ∈R × R,
(a, b) ∈Cr where
r =
√
a2 + b2).
In this instance, the partition C contains an inﬁnite number of sets and each
set Cr, except for C0, contains an inﬁnite number of elements. The equivalence
relation R on R × R corresponding to the partition C is deﬁned as follows:
Let (x, y) and (u, v) be points in the plane. Deﬁne
((x, y), (u, v)) ∈R ⇔x2 + y2 = u2 + v2.
That is, (x, y) is R-equivalent to (u, v) if and only if (x, y) and (u, v) both lie
on the same circle whose center is at the origin.
By Theorem 4.19 an equivalence relation R on a set A yields a partition of
the set A. The next theorem, which is the converse of Theorem 4.19, states
that a partition of a set yields an equivalence relation on the set.
Theorem 4.20
Let P be a partition of a nonempty set A. For x, y ∈A
deﬁne the set R as follows:
(x, y) ∈R ⇔there exists an S ∈P such that x ∈S and y ∈S.
Then R is an equivalence relation on A.

Relations
187
Proof: Let a ∈A. Since A = S
S∈P S, there exists an S ∈P such that a ∈S.
Consequently, (a, a) ∈R and R is reﬂexive.
Suppose (a, b) ∈R. By deﬁnition a, b ∈S for some S ∈P. Obviously,
b, a ∈S and therefore (b, a) ∈R. Thus, R is symmetric.
Assume (a, b) ∈R and (b, c) ∈R. By deﬁnition there exists an S ∈P such
that a, b ∈S and there exists a T ∈P such that b, c ∈T. Since b ∈S ∩T,
S ∩T ̸= ∅, which by (2) in the deﬁnition of a partition implies S = T. Thus,
a, b, c ∈S and therefore (a, c) ∈R. That is, R is transitive.
■
From Theorems 4.19 and 4.20 it follows that a partition of a nonempty set
A is equivalent to an equivalence relation on the set A. Consequently, in any
given situation we may select and use the concept which most readily suits
our needs.

4.5
Chapter Review
Deﬁnitions
Let A, B, C, and D be sets. A relation from A to B is any subset of
A × B. In particular, when B = A, a relation R from A to A is called a
relation on A.
The domain of a relation R from A to B is the set
Dom(R) = {x ∈A | (∃y ∈B)((x, y) ∈R)}.
The range of a relation R from A to B is the set
Rng(R) = {y ∈B | (∃x ∈A)((x, y) ∈R)}.
If R is a relation from A to B, then the inverse relation from B to A is
the relation
R−1 = {(x, y) ∈B × A | (y, x) ∈R}.
The identity relation on A is the set
IA = {(x, x) ∈A × A | x ∈A}.
Let R be a relation from A to B and let S be a relation from C to D.
The relation R equals S, which is denoted by R = S, if and only if A = C,
B = D, and [(x, y) ∈R ⇔(x, y) ∈S].
Let R be a relation from A to B and let S be a relation from B to C. The
composition of S and R is the relation
S ◦R = {(a, c) ∈A × C | (∃b ∈B)[((a, b) ∈R) ∧((b, c) ∈S)]}.
A set of numbers S is ordered if there exists a subset of positive numbers
Sp which satisfy the axioms:
O1. (Trichotomy Law)
For all x ∈S exactly one of the following three
statements is true:
x = 0,
x ∈Sp,
−x ∈Sp.
O2. For all x, y ∈Sp,
x + y ∈Sp.
O3. For all x, y ∈Sp,
x · y ∈Sp.
The number x is negative if and only if −x is positive.

190
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
The order relation < (read “is less than”) is deﬁned by x < y ⇔y −x is
positive.
The order relation > (read “is greater than”) is deﬁned by x > y ⇔x−y
is positive.
The order relation ≤(read “is less than or equal to”) is deﬁned by
x ≤y ⇔x < y or x = y.
The order relation ≥(read “is greater than or equal to”) is deﬁned by
x ≥y ⇔x > y or x = y.
Let A be a nonempty set of real numbers. The number m ∈A is the least
element of A (smallest element of A or minimum of A) if and only if
for every x ∈A, m ≤x. The number M ∈A is the greatest element of A
(largest element of A or maximum of A) if and only if for every x ∈A,
M ≥x.
A nonempty set A of real numbers is well-ordered if and only if every
nonempty subset of A has a least element.
Let A be a subset of the real numbers R. The number m ∈R is a lower
bound for A ⇔m ≤x for all x ∈A.
The number m ∈R is the greatest lower bound for A (inﬁmum of A)
(i) if m is a lower bound for A and (ii) if ℓis a lower bound for A, then m ≥ℓ.
The number u ∈R is an upper bound for A if u ≥x for all x ∈A.
The number u ∈R is the least upper bound for A (supremum of A)
(i) if u is an upper bound for A and (ii) if t is an upper bound for A, then
u ≤t.
The set A is bounded if it has a lower bound and an upper bound.
A ﬁeld F with addition + and multiplication · which is ordered by the
relation < is a complete ordered ﬁeld if and only if every nonempty subset
of F which has an upper bound has a least upper bound in F.
Let R be a relation on a set A.
R is a reﬂexive relation on A if and only if (∀x ∈A)[(x, x) ∈R].
R is a symmetric relation on A if and only if
(∀x, y ∈A)[((x, y) ∈R) ⇒((y, x) ∈R)].
R is a transitive relation on A if and only if
(∀x, y, z ∈A)[(((x, y) ∈R) ∧((y, z) ∈R)) ⇒((x, z) ∈R)].
A relation R is an equivalence relation on A if and only if R is reﬂexive,
symmetric, and transitive on A.
A relation R on a set A is not reﬂexive ⇔(∃x ∈A)((x, x) /∈R).
A relation R on a set A is not symmetric ⇔
(∃x, y ∈A)[((x, y) ∈R) ∧((y, x) /∈R)].
A relation R on a set A is not transitive ⇔
(∃x, y, z ∈A)[((x, y) ∈R) ∧((y, z) ∈R) ∧((x, z) /∈R)].

Relations
191
Let R be an equivalence relation on the nonempty set A. For x ∈A, the
equivalence class of x determined by the relation R is the set
[x]R = {y ∈A | (x, y) ∈R}.
The set of all equivalence classes is called A modulo R (or A mod R) and
is denoted by
A/R = {[x]R | x ∈A}.
Let A be a nonempty set and let A be a collection of subsets of A. The
collection of sets A is a partition of A if and only if
(1) X ∈A ⇒X ̸= ∅
(2) X ∈A and Y ∈A ⇒either X = Y or X ∩Y = ∅
(3) S
X∈A X = A
