Exercises 7.2
1.
Proof: We prove that f is a one-to-one function by cases. Suppose n1
and n2 are integers and for deﬁniteness that n1 ≤n2.
Case 1 If n1 ≤n2 ≤0 and f(n1) = f(n2), then 1 −2n1 = 1 −2n2,
which implies n1 = n2.
Case 2 If n1 ≤0 < n2 and f(n1) = f(n2), then 1 −2n1 = 2n2, which
implies n1 + n2 = 1/2. This is a contradiction, because the sum of two
integers is an integer.
Case 3 If 0 < n1 ≤n2 and f(n1) = f(n2), then 2n1 = 2n2, which
implies n1 = n2.
Consequently, the function f is one-to-one.
Suppose m ∈N. If m is even, then m = 2n for some n ∈N ⊂Z. If
m is odd, then m −1 is even and m −1 = 2k for some k ∈N ∪{0}.
Therefore, m = 1−2(−k) where −k is a negative integer or zero. Hence,
f is onto, and therefore f is a one-to-one correspondence from Z onto
N.
3.
Proof: Let P(n) be the statement “If A is a denumerable set and if B
is a ﬁnite set with |B| = n, then A ∪B is a denumerable set.”
(i) For n = 1, the statement P(n) is “If A is a denumerable set and if
B = {x}, then A ∪B is a denumerable set.” By Theorem 7.14, this
statement is true.
(ii) Suppose for some natural number n the statement P(n) is true. Let
A be a denumerable set and let B be a ﬁnite set with |B| = n+1. Since B
has n+1 elements, B is nonempty and there exists some x ∈B. The set
A∪B = [A∪(B−{x})]∪{x}. By Theorem 7.10, the set B−{x} is ﬁnite
and |B −{x}| = |B|−1 = n+1−1 = n. Hence, the set A∪(B −{x}) is
the union of the denumerable set A and the ﬁnite set B−{x}, which has
n elements. By the induction hypothesis, A ∪(B −{x}) is denumerable
and by Theorem 7.14, the set A∪(B −{x})]∪{x} is a denumerable set.
That is, the statement P(n + 1) is true and by induction the statement
P(n) is true for all natural numbers.

Proof: We prove this theorem by contradiction. Let A be an uncount-

B is countable, either (1) B is ﬁnite or (2) B is denumerable.

17. Proof: Suppose that < an > and < bn > are bounded sequences. By
deﬁnition, there exist positive real numbers A and B such that |an| ≤A
and |bn| ≤B for all n ∈N.
(i) By the triangle inequality, for all n ∈N, we have
|an + bn| ≤|an| + |bn| ≤A + B.
That is, the sequence < an + bn > is bounded.
(ii) Also, by the triangle inequality, for all n ∈N,
|an −bn| = |an + (−bn)| ≤|an| + | −bn| = |an| + |bn| ≤A + B.
Therefore, the sequence < an −bn > is bounded.
(iii) For all n ∈N, we have |anbn| = |an||bn| ≤AB. Therefore, the
sequence < anbn > is bounded.

7.
Proof: We prove by induction that an < 5/3 for all n ∈N. Let P(n) be
the statement “an < 5/3.” The statement P(1) is 1 < 5/3, which is true.
Now, assume that P(n) is true for some natural number n. That is, we
assume that an < 5/3 for some n ∈N. From the recursive deﬁnition,
we have an+1 = (an + 5)/4 < (5/3 + 5)/4 = 5/3. Hence, the statement
P(n + 1) is true and an < 5/3 for all n ∈N. That is, the sequence
< an > is bounded above by 5/3.
Next, we prove by induction that the sequence < an > is monotone
increasing. Let Q(n) be the statement “an ≤an+1.” The statement
Q(1) is 1 ≤3/2, which is true.
Now, we assume that Q(n) is true
for some natural number n. That is, we assume that an ≤an+1 for
some n ∈N. Adding 5 to the last inequality and then dividing by 4,
we obtain (an + 5)/4 ≤(an+1 + 5)/4. From the recursive deﬁnition, it
follows that an+1 ≤an+2. Therefore, the statement Q(n+1) is true, and
by mathematical induction the sequence < an > is monotone increasing.
Hence, the sequence is bounded below by a1 = 1.
By Theorem 8.9 (the monotone convergence theorem), the sequence
< an > converges to some real number A.
Taking the limit of the
recursive deﬁnition of an+1 as n approaches inﬁnity, we ﬁnd A = (A +
5)/4. Solving for A, we obtain A = 5/3.
9. Proof: We prove by induction that an > 1/2 for all n ∈N.
Let
P(n) be the statement “an > 1/2.” The statement P(1) is 2 > 1/2,
which is true. Assume that the statement P(n) is true for some natural
number n. That is, assume that an > 1/2 for some n ∈N. Therefore,
1 > 1/(2an) and −1/(2an) > −1. From the recursive deﬁnition, we see
that an+1 = 2−1/(2an) > 2−1 = 1 > 1/2. Hence, the sequence < an >
is bounded below by 1/2.
Now, we prove by induction that the sequence < an > is monotone
decreasing. Let Q(n) be the statement “an+1 ≤an.” The statement
Q(1) is a2 ≤a1, which is true, since a1 = 2 and a2 = 2 −1/4 = 7/4.
Assume for some natural number n that an+1 ≤an.
Then 1/an ≤
1/an+1. Because an+1 > 1/2 > 0, we have −1/(2an+1) ≤−1/(2an),
which implies 2 −1/(2an+1) ≤2 −1/(2an). Thus, from the recursive
deﬁnition an+2 ≤an+1 and the statement Q(n+1) is true. By induction,
the sequence < an > is monotone decreasing and bounded above by
a1 = 2.
By Theorem 8.9, the sequence < an > converges to some real number A.
Taking the limit of the recursive deﬁnition for an+1 as n tends to inﬁnity,
we ﬁnd A satisﬁes the equation A = 2 −1/(2A) or 2A2 −4A + 1 = 0.
Hence, A = 1 ±
√
2/2. Since 1/2 < an < 2 for all n ∈N, we conclude
A = 1 +
√
2/2.
11. Proof: First, we prove that an > 3 for all n ∈N. Let P(n) be the
statement “an > 3.” The statement P(1) is true, because a1 = 4 > 3.

Suppose for some natural number n that an > 3. Adding 6 to this
inequality, we obtain 6 + an > 9, which implies an+1 = √6 + an > 3.
Thus, the statement P(n + 1) is true. Hence, by induction, an > 3 for
all n ∈N and the sequence < an > is bounded below by 3.
Next, we prove that an > an+1 for all n ∈N. Since an > 3, we have
an−3 > 0 and an+2 > 0. Therefore, 0 < (an−3)(an +2) = a2
n−an−6.
Solving for a2
n, we ﬁnd a2
n > 6+an, which implies an > √6 + an = an+1
by the recursive deﬁnition. Therefore, the sequence < an > is monotone
decreasing and bounded above by a1 = 4.
By Theorem 8.9, the sequence < an > converges to a real number
A. From the recursive deﬁnition, we see that A satisﬁes the equation
A = √6 + A. Squaring this equation, we ﬁnd A2 = 6 + A.
Hence,
A2 −A −6 = (A −3)(A + 2) = 0 and A = 3 or A = −2.
Since
3 < an < 4 for all n ∈N, we conclude an →3.

11. Proof: Let e be the identity of the group G and let a−1 be the inverse
of a. Consider y = ba−1 ∈G. By associativity, we have ya = (ba−1)a =
b(aa−1) = be = b. Hence, y is one solution of ya = b. Suppose that y1
and y2 ̸= y1 are both solutions of the equation ya = b. Then y1a = y2a
and by the right cancellation law y1 = y2, which contradicts y2 ̸= y1.
Therefore, y = ba−1 is the unique solution of ya = b.
13. Proof: First, we assume that G is an abelian group. Let a, b ∈G.
By Theorem 9.4, we have (1) (ab)−1 = b−1a−1. Since G is abelian,
(2) b−1a−1 = a−1b−1. From (1) and (2), we see that (ab)−1 = a−1b−1.
Next, suppose that (ab)−1 = a−1b−1 for all a, b ∈G.
Let e be the
identity element of G. Since a, b ∈G, the elements a−1, b−1, a−1b−1,
(ab)−1 are elements of G. By deﬁnition of inverse (1) (ab)(ab)−1 = e.
By hypothesis (2) (ab)−1 = a−1b−1. Substituting (2) into (1), we obtain
(3) (ab)a−1b−1 = e. Multiplying (3) on the right by b yields aba−1b−1b =
eb or (4) aba−1 = b. Then, multiplying (4) on the right by a, we obtain
ab = ba. Therefore, G is abelian.

5.
Proof: Let G be a group with identity e and let H and K be subgroups
of G. By Theorem 9.6, e ∈H and e ∈K. Therefore, e ∈H ∩K and
H ∩K is a nonempty set. Suppose that a, b ∈H ∩K. Then a, b ∈H
and a, b ∈K. Since H and K are subgroups of G, the element b−1 ∈H
and b−1 ∈K by Theorem 9.6. Since H and K are groups, ab−1 ∈H
and ab−1 ∈K. Thus, ab−1 ∈H ∩K and, by Theorem 9.7, the set H ∩K
is a subgroup of G.
7.
Proof: By deﬁnition, H is a subgroup of the group (K, ◦) if and only if
H is a subset of K and (H, ◦) is a group. Likewise, K is a subgroup of
the group (G, ◦) if and only if K is a subset of G and (K, ◦) is a group.
Since H ⊆K and K ⊆G implies H ⊆G and since (H, ◦) is a group,
(H, ◦) is subgroup of (G, ◦) by deﬁnition.

Proof: Let G be a group with identity e and let a be any element of
G. Since ea = ae, we have e ∈C(a), and therefore C(a) is nonempty.
Suppose that g ∈C(a). Then (1) ga = ag. Multiplying equation (1) on
the left by g−1, we obtain

388
Introduction to Mathematical Proofs
g−1(ga) = (g−1g)a = ea = a = g−1ag.
Multiplying a = g−1ag on the right by g−1, we ﬁnd
ag−1 = (g−1ag)g−1 = (g−1a)(gg−1) = (g−1a)e = g−1a.
Therefore g−1 ∈C(a). Now, suppose that g, h ∈C(a). Then ga = ag
and ha = ah. Computing, we ﬁnd
(gh)a = g(ha) = g(ah) = (ga)h = (ag)h = a(gh).
Hence, gh ∈C(a) and, by Theorem 9.8, the set C(a) is a subgroup of
G.

That is, K is a nonempty set. Suppose that b, c ∈K. Then there is
an h1 ∈H such that b = a−1h1a and there is an h2 ∈H such that
